QUESTIONS ON NOTICE - ANSWERS

No. 2 - Plan to Address Youth Offending
[11.06 a.m.]

 [Bookmark: _Hlk215652764] The following answer to a question on notice was given.

Ms THOMAS question to LEADER for the GOVERMENT in the LEGISLATIVE COUNCIL, Ms RATTRAY.

ANSWER

Will the Government develop a plan to address youth offending?

Our government remains committed to redesigning the Youth Justice System to provide for better outcomes for young people and ensuring safe communities.  [Bookmark: Phr6_O=50.9_D=2.4_C=0.82]  [Bookmark: Phr7_O=64.1_D=28.1_C=0.89] They will work to continue to focus efforts on stopping young people offending and diverting young people from the Youth Justice System. Tasmanian Government is delivering on its commitment to reform the Tasmanian Youth Justice System.

 [Bookmark: Phr8_O=92.4_D=29.8_C=0.81] Youth Justice Blueprint 2024 to 2034 provides the clear pathway to achieve successful reform. Also, the Youth Justice Diversionary Services Framework sets out requirements for funded diversionary services in Tasmania aligned to the Youth Justice Model of Care, contemporary evidence and best practice.

Through the 2025-26 Budget, funding of $1.6 million over 3 years has been allocated to build  [Bookmark: Phr9_O=122.5_D=30.0_C=0.82] Youth Justice workforce capability The government is committed to closing the Ashley Youth Detention Centre as soon as possible and developing a new facility based on therapeutic rights based and child focus model of care. To deliver on this commitment, a total of $155.3 million is allocated to building the youth justice facility in the south of the state at Pontville. The new youth justice facility at Pontville is being designed to deliver a child [Bookmark: Phr10_O=152.5_D=28.7_C=0.81]  centred and therapeutic model of care that addresses the individual needs and risk factors of young offenders while keeping the community safe.

The commitment to a proactive Tasmanian Police presence on the beat in Glenorchy, Claremont and Moonah. This is an operational matter for Tasmania Police and dependent upon available resources noting other competing demands in the community. [Bookmark: Phr11_O=181.6_D=16.2_C=0.74]  Continuing task force activity is not always sustainable but is an option for local managers to consider if it is deemed necessary and achievable.

Recently, Tasmanian Police stepped up its focus on reducing anti-social behaviour  [Bookmark: Phr12_O=198.0_D=16.6_C=0.79] and retail crime in the Glenorchy Municipality launching Taskforce Respect, a community campaign involving high visibility patrols in the CBD and shopping areas. Taskforce Respect also covers the Moonah and Claremont areas.
 [Bookmark: Phr13_O=215.1_D=15.5_C=0.78] Tasmanian Police continues to work in partnership with Glenorchy City Council, businesses and stakeholders such as Metro Tasmania to prevent, resolve and investigate issues of crime and anti-social behaviour that occur in public spaces.

 [Bookmark: Phr14_O=232.0_D=28.9_C=0.83] Question B on urgent legislative change. Tasmania's framework, under the Security and Investigations Agents Act 2002 and associated licencing and training requirements, is designed to provide an appropriate balance between maintaining public safety and ensuring that the use of coercive powers remains within the remit of police and authorised officers. The government will consider any  [Bookmark: Phr15_O=261.1_D=27.4_C=0.82] specific proposals put forward for legislative amendments.

Question C on the increased investment in surveillance technology, including CCTV monitoring systems. In September 2024, a collaboration between the City of Hobart and Tasmanian Police saw the installation of a portal at the Hobart Police Station allowing officers to directly access  [Bookmark: Phr16_O=289.3_D=1.0_C=0.82] CCTV footage  [Bookmark: Phr17_O=290.7_D=14.3_C=0.89] from about 330 camera views within the Hobart municipality. This has been a successful tool for managing active incidents and extracting evidence after an event, as well as guiding officers on the beat and monitoring the welfare of individuals. Tasmania Police is an end user of the private/public CCTV. However, a central CCTV monitoring system and 24/7 security monitoring would be a matter for local government. 

In answer to question D, regarding a review of sentencing and diversion program outcomes under the Youth Justice Act of 1997 to evaluate whether they are meeting the needs and expectations of the community, victims and young people, the Tasmanian government is actively working to deliver a youth justice system that supports the diversion of children and young people from offending and risk-taking behaviours, with a comprehensive review of the Youth Justice Act of 1997 underway.

The review of the act will ensure that it is updated to reflect a contemporary youth justice system in line with the Youth Justice Blueprint of 2024-2034, including increases to the minimum age of criminal responsibility and detention. The objective of the act review is to support a contemporary best-practice youth justice model in Tasmania to ensure children and young people engaging in harmful behaviours and offending are supported in a manner that best promotes their safety, wellbeing and rights, encourages positive social development and strengthens protective factors, greater community safety by embedding responses that are evidence-based and aimed at addressing the root causes of harmful behaviour, and meeting identified needs. The legislative framework is more user-friendly, easy to administer and is fit for purpose. 

The Tasmanian government is taking a rights-based approach, including the rights of the child and young person, and the rights of the victim and the community, and addressing the consequences of behaviours through restorative and therapeutic youth justice approaches. A working group with representatives from the Department of Health, Department of Police, Fire and Emergency Management, Department of Justice and the Department for Education, Children and Young People is meeting regularly to inform the act review policy considerations.

Consultation will commence shortly with key stakeholders including children and young people with lived experience of the justice system, Aboriginal communities, and victims of crime by children and young people. Through this collaborative approach, the views reflected lived experience, service needs and support youth justice system change.

Question E: the review of youth bail laws, with reference to Section 24C of the Youth Justice Act of 1997. The Commission of Inquiry into the Tasmanian Government’s Responses to Child Sexual Abuse in Institutional Settings made multiple recommendations relating to bail for children and young people, as well as relating to broader youth justice issues, such as raising the minimum age of criminal responsibility and the types of government facilities that should be available for children and young people. There is a large volume of work underway in relation to these other aspects of reform of the youth justice system. 

Question F: legislation providing for Workplace Protection Orders. Workplace Protection Orders represent a new approach introduced in the Australian Capital Territory (ACT) and the Tasmanian government is carefully assessing the relevance and suitability of such measures within Tasmania's legal and workplace safety frameworks.

Question G: minimum packaging standards of knives and other sharp objects and the ban on importation of knives and sharp objects by minors. The government is progressing legislation to ban the sale of knives to children and young people. As the legislation is being developed, consideration will be given to including retailer responsibilities such as signage requirements and secure storage of knives in retail businesses. The proposed legislation will be consulted on through the usual public consultation process.

Question H is regarding the case study research into 30 youth offenders with 50 or more police charges in 2024. The Tasmanian government's youth justice system reform policy decisions are directly informed by evidence-based work across several agencies, including data analysis and review of best-practice models. The Youth Justice Blueprint and Youth Justice Model of Care ensures that service delivery is child-and-young-person centred and there is a shared understanding of what a therapeutic approach to youth justice means. 

The Tasmanian government continues to apply an evidence-based approach to better understand the systemic and social factors that influence youth offending and risk-taking behaviours. This work is underway and ongoing, and includes case study analysis of young people with high-level offending behaviour and the circumstances of a cohort of young people who have interacted with custodial youth justice.

The available data and information relating to a specific cohort of young people's education, engagement, Tasmania Police interactions, child safety and youth justice is assisting to build a deeper understanding of offending behaviours to inform policy reform. 

Question I is regarding increased investment in early intervention programs: the Tasmanian government is committed to delivering a youth justice system that achieves better outcomes for children and young people and their families and keeps our community safe. The Tasmanian government has invested $3.7 million over three years into supporting JCPs Youth BEAST program, which aims to support the diversion of at-risk young people from offending behaviours. The Tasmanian government is investing a total of $7.5 million over three years to support innovative local initiatives and partnerships that target the root causes of youth offending in local communities, divert young people out of the youth justice system and provide support to young people on bail. This investment delivers on the commitment to the commission of inquiry recommendation that the Tasmanian government develop a youth justice diversion reservices framework and begin delivering programmes under the framework by 2025, which was recommendation 12.13.

The Youth Justice Blueprint 2024 to 2034 sets the direction for a contemporary youth justice system in Tasmania. The Youth Justice Blueprint has a focus on diversion away from the youth justice system through a range of early intervention supports for families, diversion programs and community-based alternatives to custodial remand. 

As part of the blueprint commitment, the Tasmanian government has released the Youth Justice Model of Care and Implementation Plan. The implementation of the model of care is underway, beginning with the development of a self-assessment tool to support organisations providing youth justice services to assess their current activities against the model of care and make changes as appropriate. The diversionary services framework provides guidance for the procurement of services consistent with the model of care.

A youth after-hours diversion service is being delivered through two contracted organisations: Catholic Care and Anglicare. From the Glenorchy, Bridgewater, Hobart and Bellerive police stations, the aim of the Youth After-Hours Diversion Service (YADS) pilot is to divert young people from the youth justice system through police bail, which can reduce the potential for them to be remanded at Ashley Youth Detention Centre with the aim of diverting young people away from offending behaviours.

The review of 54 Reasons bail support and transition from detention program has been completed, and a response is being prepared to the review's recommendations, including opportunities to expand the services.

Work on the Aboriginal Youth Justice Strategy - the strategy - is occurring in parallel to engagement on the suite of youth justice reforms according to the direction outlined in the Youth Justice Blueprint. The strategy is being developed in partnership with Aboriginal people and organisations, which is underpinned by self-determination and focuses on prevention, early intervention and diversion strategies for Aboriginal children and young people. Have your Say Posters and pamphlets were distributed in April 2025 to the community to provide information and prompt discussion as part of ongoing community engagement about the Aboriginal Youth Justice Strategy.

Ms RATTRAY - The answer to question (J) - We recognise that some children and young people alternate flexible learning provisions are required to enable access, participation and engagement in education. This includes Tasmanian E-school, support schools, T4 re engagement programs and the recognition of part-time enrolments [Bookmark: Phr8_O=123.1_D=29.5_C=0.80] . All T4 engagement programs have a social worker and school psychology allocation to provide support and intervention as required.

Young people exiting AYDC are supported to return to the school they were previously enrolled at or to seek a suitable supported alternative education pathway, in addition to the supports provided by schools and the Department of Education, Children and Young People. The Office of the Education Registrar [Bookmark: Phr9_O=153.6_D=14.8_C=0.79]  provides an additional layer of support to students and families who face challenges with non-attendance through the compulsory conciliation conference. A conciliation conference is run by an experienced and independent conciliator with the aim is to re-engage the student with their education under the framework of restorative practise.

The conciliator is required to report to the Registrar of Education Education on the outcome of the conciliation. If there has been an agreement reached, the registrar may make a formal requirement that the parties do these things [Bookmark: Phr11_O=196.2_D=3.4_C=0.60]  they agreed to. If there is not an agreement [Bookmark: Phr12_O=200.0_D=30.0_C=0.74]  reached, then the registrar will consider the conciliator's report and make a determination based on the report and what happened in the conciliation and make a formal requirement. If a requirement of a registrar is not complied with, then the registrar may decide to refer the matter to the principal officer, non-attendance for a compulsory school in order a CSO to be made. Failure to comply with the CSO is an offence under the act and it may be referred to the Magistrate's [Bookmark: Phr13_O=230.1_D=10.5_C=0.74]  Court, and a charge may issue. This could result in a criminal record and a fine or community service order.

Question 13. Civil litigation and employment investigations are different processes. Employment investigations are administrative processes conducted by employing agencies. Civil litigation is a legal process conducted in courts. Both processes operate  [Bookmark: Phr4_O=53.9_D=4.4_C=0.80] for different purposes, with different thresholds, different evidentiary requirements and can occur  [Bookmark: Phr5_O=58.6_D=3.1_C=0.85] at different times.

Question 14.  [Bookmark: Phr6_O=63.2_D=29.4_C=0.81] The National Redress Scheme provides support to redress applicants via No More, a nationwide free and confidential service which supports victim/survivors of childhood abuse through the provision of trauma informed legal assistance, financial counselling, social support and specialised cultural support for First Nations people. 

In addition to this national support service, a number of Tasmanian non-government organisations also offer support  [Bookmark: Phr7_O=92.8_D=13.4_C=0.78] to people interacting with the National Redress Scheme, including Relationships Australia, Sexual Assault Support Services and the Blue Knot Foundation and the South East Tasmanian Aboriginal Corporation (SETAC [Bookmark: Phr8_O=107.9_D=14.5_C=0.62] ), Neena Lena [TBC 11.32.35 a.m.] Tasmanian Aboriginal Redress Support Service.

The Department of Justice can assist National Redress Scheme participants who have accepted an offer of redress with a counselling and psychological care  [Bookmark: Phr9_O=123.2_D=29.7_C=0.86] component. However, the department does not have access to the information required to assist an individual victim/survivor until confirmation of their offer acceptance has been received from the scheme. It can take up to six weeks for the scheme to process an acceptance letter and convey this to the relevant jurisdiction. While the process is underway, victim/survivors residing in Tasmania can access free legal and/or financial assistance through independent organisations referenced above.

 [Bookmark: Phr10_O=153.0_D=29.6_C=0.80] Victim/survivors can also receive support under a mental health care plan prepared by general practitioner. 

Question 15. The Department of Justice notes that Alicia was a witness before the Commission of Inquiry into the Tasmanian government's responses to child sexual abuse in institutional settings (the commission of inquiry), and Alicia's evidence was tested and considered by the commission of inquiry.

 [Bookmark: Phr11_O=182.9_D=29.7_C=0.79] Question 16. The commission of inquiry heard and examined evidence to support its important work. The commission of inquiry's final report, titled Who Was Looking After Me Prioritising the Safety of Tasmania's Children was delivered to the Governor of Tasmania on 31 August 2023 and tabled in parliament on 26 September 2023. 

The commission of inquiry's final report contains the commission's process, reasoning and  [Bookmark: Phr12_O=212.9_D=3.5_C=0.55] findings.

Question 17.  [Bookmark: Phr13_O=217.6_D=12.1_C=0.80] The COI identified a high volume of serious allegations at AYDC involving current and former employees across both historical and recent periods.

 [Bookmark: Phr14_O=230.5_D=8.9_C=0.83] It is acknowledged that a number of these ED5 matters with historical allegations have been outstanding for a considerable time.  [Bookmark: Phr15_O=239.6_D=2.5_C=0.89] This is because this is difficult work.  [Bookmark: Phr16_O=243.1_D=29.5_C=0.82] They are the most serious of allegations and new allegations continue to emerge. 

Every employment-based investigation relating to the AYDC is treated with the seriousness demanded of the subject matter, including the need to bring such investigations and processes to a conclusion whilst ensuring the protection of the children is at the forefront whilst balancing natural justice and due process with transparency.

 [Bookmark: Phr17_O=273.8_D=29.0_C=0.84] Question 18. The department is unaware of an inconsistent approach to suspending staff. The department takes allegations of breaches of the state service code of conduct (the code) very seriously. The department has robust governance arrangements and detailed processes in place when allegations against current employees are received. All employees who are subject to allegations of child sexual abuse arising from duty.

The process that is followed with all allegations involves the following:

Allegations are referred to the secretary to consider whether they are reasonable grounds to believe that a breach of the code may have occurred and whether suspension under Employment Direction No. 4 is in the public interest.

The secretary has discretion under ED5 whether to investigate all alleged breaches and whether or not to appoint an investigator. Such decisions are influenced by the seriousness of the alleged conduct and what evidence is available at the time of that decision.

If an investigation is commenced and an investigator appointed, the secretary is provided with an investigation report prepared by an independent investigator. Following the consideration of the report and the employees response to the report, the secretary makes a determination with regard to a breach of the Code and whether the alleged conduct may also comprise reportable conduct under the Child and Youth Safe Organisations Act 2023.

If a determination of a breach of the code is made, a range of sanctions may be imposed in accordance with the State Service Act 2000. If a finding of reportable conduct is made, that is reported to the office of the independent regulator for its consideration.

If a code process results in a decision that an employee has breached the code of conduct, the available sanctions include:

counselling,
a reprimand,
deductions from salary by way of fine,
reductions in salary,
reassignment of duties,
reduction in classification and
termination of employment.

Only the secretary may impose the sanction of termination of employment subject to consultation with the State Service Management Office.

A detailed determination is provided to the respondent employee, including the reasons for any finding that the employee has breached the code. A letter of outcome explaining the process and thanking key witnesses for their participation is provided once the outcome is communicated to the employee.

Confidentiality is an important element of all code matters and all responsible efforts are made to otherwise keep the details of any investigation confidential, both to maintain the integrity of the process and so the privacy of these involved is maintained.

(19)	A key feature of the Registration to Work with Vulnerable People scheme is the ongoing monitoring of registered persons.

When the registrar receives new and relevant information relating to a registered person, the registrar will conduct an additional risk assessment on the person's suitability to continue to hold registration.

As part of the risk assessment, the registrar may continue to receive and/or request further information from agencies relating to that person. The registrar uses this information collectively to assess the level of risk a person presents to vulnerable people.

Under the Registration to Work with Vulnerable People Act 2013, the registrar has grounds to suspend a person's registration while they are under an additional risk assessment. 

The registrar will suspend a person's registration when having considered the available information if they form the belief that the registered person likely presents an unacceptable risk of harm to vulnerable people. The purpose of suspending a person's registration is to remove that person from regulated activities and mitigate any risks to vulnerable people while the risk assessment process is completed.

The decision to suspend is made on the specific information, both contemporary and historical, relating to that registered person and resulting identified risks. Given the unique nature of each risk assessment, comparison should not be made between individual matters and their respective outcomes.

(20)	Suspension of staff under Employment Direction No. 4 is a lawful and necessary measure used when it is in the public interest to temporarily remove an employee from the workplace.

Such action may be taken, for example, to protect the integrity of investigations, ensure the safety and wellbeing of children and staff, and uphold public confidence in the state service.

In such circumstances, suspended employees are restricted from workplace contact to prevent interference with witnesses, evidence or ongoing inquiries.

Employment Direction No. 5 outlines procedures for investigating alleged breaches of the code of conduct, including trauma-informed and child-safety-focused practises. These may involve limiting contact between suspended staff and colleagues to protect vulnerable individuals and maintai [Bookmark: Phr2_O=29.3_D=15.2_C=0.80] n procedural fairness. Importantly, suspended employees retain access to support services, including the Employee Assistance Program, and are kept informed through the appropriate channels.

 [Bookmark: Phr3_O=45.1_D=0.6_C=0.54]  [Bookmark: Phr4_O=52.3_D=1.6_C=0.39] Question  [Bookmark: Phr5_O=54.9_D=28.8_C=0.81] 21. Employees, subject to processes associated with allegations of wrongdoing in their employment, are encouraged to raise concerns and any specific supports they require through the process.

 [Bookmark: Phr7_O=98.8_D=29.5_C=0.83] Answer to Question 22. The government acknowledges that investigations and suspensions can be deeply challenging for individuals, particularly in high-pressure environments like youth justice. Allegations involving child safety must be treated seriously and processes are guided by employment directions and the principles of procedural fairness. While the government does not comment on individual cases, it recognises the importance of supporting staff [Bookmark: Phr8_O=128.8_D=24.4_C=0.82]  who are cleared of wrongdoing. Options such as restorative processes and access to support services may be considered where appropriate. 

Answer to Question 23. The Department for Education, Children and Young People, in addition to the general supports referred to in response to Question 21, in April 2025 engaged [Bookmark: Phr9_O=154.5_D=5.9_C=0.79]  a contracted service provider to deliver a pilot program of support for suspended employees [Bookmark: Phr10_O=160.9_D=29.7_C=0.73] , including wellbeing checks, personal counselling and engagement strategies and advice for managers in how to support their staff who are suspended. All suspended employees were individually contacted in May 2025 with advice about how to access support from the pilot program. Employees have also recently received information about the department's new employee wellbeing connect services which [Bookmark: Phr11_O=190.8_D=29.9_C=0.81]  further improve on the previous support offerings. 

Answer to Question 24. The Tasmanian government addresses vexatious or false allegations through procedural safeguards embedded in the State Service Act 2000, employment directions and the integrity commission's misconduct framework. Investigations must uphold procedural fairness, including preliminary assessments to test credibility and intent.

 [Bookmark: Phr12_O=221.2_D=19.9_C=0.82] While Tasmania does not have stand-alone legislation targeting vexatious complaints against public servants, current frameworks allow agencies to manage such conduct appropriately, ensuring both accountability and protection from misuse of complaint processes.

No. 9 - Investment in Sport in Tasmania

[11.47 a.m.]
The following answer to a question on notice was given.

Ms THOMAS question to MINISTER for SPORT, Mr DUIGAN

Can the Government provide a breakdown of its investment in sport in Tasmania in the last ten years, detailing calculations and total funding invested in the following:

professional sport - including team and individual athlete sponsorship, events and funding for any other operational purpose.

elite sport - including athlete development, team and individual athlete sponsorship, and funding for any other operational purpose;

community sport – including sport development programs and funding for any other operational purpose;

facilities for professional sport;

facilities for elite sport;

facilities for community in each region of Tasmania (South, North, North-West); and

Will the Government commit to developing a community sport infrastructure and investment program for each region of Tasmania (south, north, north-west) by the end of 2026?

ANSWER

Tasmanian government investment into sport in Tasmania occurs through various areas of the Department of State Growth including Active Tasmania, Infrastructure Tasmania, Events Tasmania and the Tasmanian Institute of Sport. To provide accurate data across all sport related investment, the department has included committed investments from 2021 to 2026, unless otherwise specified. Data prior to 2020-21 is not available to be accurately collated and presented in the time frame. Some of the following figures reflect committed investments, and in some cases, instalments may be paid in 2026-27 and beyond.

In sponsorship, events, development, operational A, B and C, a total investment of $328.3 million in the area of sponsorship events, development and operational purposes across professional, elite and community sporting applications. Given the various investment sources into sponsorship, events, development, and other operational purpose, the data is presented by relevant business area and then further broken down into categories to reflect the intent of the question.

Active Tasmania. Since 2020-21, a total of $38.5 million has been invested for sport development programmes and other operational programmes split as following; professional $6.4 million, community $32.1 million. The above figures include current agreements with instalments due in 2025-26 or later. Agreements not yet finalised or that remain under negotiation are not included above, however funding is included in the 2025-26 State Budget.

Events Tasmania. Since 2021, a total of $118.3 million has been invested into sporting events across the following categories Special partnerships, elite and professional. This includes items such as Hawthorn Football Club, JackJumpers, T20 World Cup. $90.2 million, major events elite/community - this includes items such as Point to Pinnacle, Dancesport championships, Emergency Service Games - $20 million [Bookmark: Phr2_O=17.1_D=10.7_C=0.84] , all other events community and this includes items such as Cadbury Marathon, Tasmanian Pickleball Championship - $8.1  million.

 [Bookmark: Phr3_O=30.1_D=27.9_C=0.75] The area of strategy, housing, infrastructure and planning, professional sport, the club funding and development agreement signed with the AFL, a total of $164 million has been committed to the Tasmanian Devils Football Club for elite sport. That is made-up of establishment funding of $20 million and operational funding of $144 million. The Tasmanian Institute of Sport elite sport from 2019-20 to 2024-25 [Bookmark: Phr4_O=58.4_D=29.7_C=0.80] , total of $7.3 million has been invested in TIS sports programs each year, excluding salaries and overheads. This funding supports the training environment and direct athlete needs, including medical, competition and other high-performance requirements.

Facilities are D, E and F. In relation to facilities, the difference between professional and elite sporting facilities is not easily identifiable. Further, there are very limited [Bookmark: Phr5_O=88.2_D=23.8_C=0.73]  facilities in the state that are dedicated to professional or elite sporting facilities. As such the following categories have been adopted - elite/professionals such as the AFL High Performance Centre, professional/community - UTAS Stadium, Silverdome, My State Bank Arena, JackJumpers High Performance Centre; community - Devonport Oval, Glenorchy Pool and Elphin Sports Centre.

 [Bookmark: Phr6_O=113.1_D=29.8_C=0.71] Silverdome - the venue is regularly used each week for community use, supported by some elite sporting and cultural events throughout the year. During 2025, netball training occurred at the Silverdome four nights per week year-round. The Launceston Cycling Club used the velodrome for training and track meets. The Tasmanian Institute of Sport utilise the facility for training and meets. Commercial and elite use in 2025 included two home JackJumpers' matches, two professional indoor rodeos, several [Bookmark: Phr7_O=143.0_D=30.0_C=0.77]  school leavers dinners, makers markets and hosting Targa Tasmania.

UTAS Stadium - the stadium is regularly used each week for community and local use, playing host to the North Launceston Bombers in the NTFA which includes training twice per week for their senior program and twice per week for the junior program and home matches for the North Launceston Footy Club. Throughout the winter Utah Stadium hosts the National Tasmania Football Association finals series including grand final weekend and the AFL Tasmania Junior [Bookmark: Phr8_O=173.2_D=3.8_C=0.70]  Pathway program trains from that venue. [Bookmark: Phr9_O=178.6_D=25.2_C=0.70]  Over summer, it hosts the Greater Northern Raiders cricket and the Under 17 National Cricket Carnival. In 2025, UTAS Stadium was used for elite sport on six occasions. one AFL pre-season match for AFL home and away season matches, one AFLW match.

Since 2021, a total of $534.6 million [Bookmark: Phr10_O=204.3_D=27.6_C=0.81]  has been invested into state sporting facilities, including additional funding for key projects included in the 2025-26 state budgets. Investment across the categories as follow - elite/professional $115 million, professional/community $224.3 million, community $195.4 million. [Bookmark: Phr11_O=232.7_D=2.5_C=0.78] 

In relation to the investment into community facilities, the [Bookmark: Phr12_O=235.6_D=29.6_C=0.78]  total of $195.4 million has been allocated across the regions as follows - south $66.3 million (34 per cent), north $69 million (35 per cent), northwest $60.1 million (31 per cent). Further, the 2025-26 state budget included funding for the Kingsborough Sports Precinct and the Tasmanian Active Infrastructure Grant program [Bookmark: Phr13_O=265.5_D=27.7_C=0.84]  and Active Tasmania will also provide a minor facilities' program this financial year. This is an additional $30.6 million which is not included in the totals outlined above.

Community sporting infrastructure projects are continuing to be delivered in partnership with the Commonwealth and local governments. The Department of State Growth published the Tasmanian Community Support Facilities portal on five September 2025 [Bookmark: Phr14_O=293.3_D=3.8_C=0.82]  capturing and displaying, as an initial phase, council owned facilities and [Bookmark: Phr15_O=297.4_D=7.6_C=0.78]  school facilities data. More facility datasets can be expanded in the portal over coming time, and this work will be considered as part of an evaluation of the first stage of this initiative. The portal provides the functionality to overlay and draw insights from a range of datasets. In this initial phase, the system can report on community sport facilities by local government area, population, census data, facility type and key information on the latest major facility upgrades where this has been provided. The portal is a key input to guide future investment of community sport infrastructure. A potential next step is to consider, in early 2026, the development of a strategic plan to provide the Tasmanian government and stakeholders with a framework to inform future investment decisions.

SPECIAL INTEREST MATTERS

Free2b Girls

[12.01 p.m.]
Ms PALMER (Rosevears - Minister for Children and Youth) - Mr President, I rise today to share a story of an inspiring initiative making a real difference in the lives of young women in my electorate of Rosevears. At the heart of the Beaconsfield Neighbourhood House is a really vibrant program called Free2b Girls. It's a space where young women come together to learn and to grow and to simply be themselves.

This program is led by Dannielle Murfet[checked], an amazing local mum of four who has more energy than you could believe. I first met Dannielle over five years ago when she was not only running a small business, Totally Mum Made, but was also lobbying for support to upgrade the Beaconsfield Primary School swimming pool. As if her life wasn't busy enough, she grabbed an opportunity to coordinate Free2b Girls, run through the Beaconsfield Neighbourhood House.

With around 15 girls involved. Free2b Girls offers a safe and supportive environment where these young women, or maybe we should say tweens, build confidence, develop skills, and form lasting friendships. With a focus on girls in rural regions across Tasmania, we see a number of groups running in communities such as St Helens, Longford and Campbell Town, and attention is given to wellbeing and nurturing, with some potential for lots of fun. As one participant put it, 'It's a good place to just be silly.' It sometimes feels like just being silly can be quite risky for young people, with the intrusion of mobile devices that have the potential to beam your life to the world. That simple phrase, 'It's a good place to just be silly,' captures the joy, the freedom and the acceptance that this program brings.

These amazing and unique West Tamar young women are learning practical skills like financial literacy. They also do some sewing and some cooking. They also had a skateboard session, where they were determined to get me on a skateboard doing laps of the Beaconsfield Community Centre, and they succeeded. I believe my attempts were a great source of amusement to them all. They're also exploring entrepreneurship by creating handmade items - hair accessories, pens and key chains - which they then go on to sell at our local markets in Beauty Point and Exeter, with the money raised to support their group.

Beyond activities, the program fosters intergenerational mentorship. It connects these girls with older women in our community who share their wisdom and encouragement. Another participant said, 'Free2b Girls has so much stuff to do and it's all age appropriate, and the volunteers are just so nice.' What I personally love about this group is how they actively give back to our community. They've made craft packs for local children, catered the ANZAC Day lunch for 90 people, and hosted a community dinner for International Women's Day, celebrating local women leaders and showing them what young women can achieve when empowered.

Their next project involves making and selling boomerang bags, promoting sustainability and demonstrating how small actions can make a big impact. The program also supports mental health through mindfulness activities like colouring sessions, giving the girls space to pause and reflect. When you have these young women saying they love Free2b Girls because, 'It makes me feel happy and more loved,' you know it's just what they need to keep their lives thriving and keep them thriving in our community.

I want to commend Dannielle Murfet for her leadership; Jane Pinnington, the centre manager of the Beaconsfield Neighbourhood House; and all the volunteers who are involved in Free2b Girls for their dedication and their vision. We know that programs like this, that connect young women not only to each other, but also to older women in their community, can create a safer environment for them. It's so important we have a nurturing, connected and confident community for future generations. It's wonderful to see these capable and compassionate young people, who feel they can be really safe just being silly like all other kids.


Scottsdale Cattle Handler - Will Hadley

[12.05 p.m.]
Ms RATTRAY (McIntyre - Leader for the Government in the Legislative Council) - Mr President, thank you. Most of the people in here will probably know the agricultural show season has just concluded across the state; the 115th Circular Head Show was the final show on the calendar. Those shows have cattle-handling as a major feature of many of those shows, particularly in the north of the state.

Today, I'd like to share with honourable members the journey of one young cattle handler who is representing our state and our country on the world stage. Will Hadley[checked], 21 years of age, a young Scottsdale local who is a part of the Team Australia who were named the overall champions of the inaugural B.E.E.F. International Competition at Farm Fair, held in Edmonton, Canada, early November this year. B.E.E.F. stands for Battle of Emerging Elite Farmer - what a title.

The competition ran for five days, which included a range of workshops including judging classes, herdsmen competitions, and fitting workshops, with Australia reigning supreme over the biggest beef breeding countries in the world. For each, judging took place where competitors were evaluated across the duration, and Team Australia ultimately came out on top.

Will's cattle-handling career started at the Scottsdale High School in year 7. I'm sorry that the Minister for Education has left the Chamber, because this is why the Scottsdale High School needs a new facility at the school farm, because look what we are achieving. There are many young people that have come through the school cattle-handling program and continue to grow as young people because of that program.

Will has gone on to establish his own stud beef herd, the Heatherfield Angus, with many successes along the way at the agricultural shows in Tasmania. I go to most of them across my electorate, and Will and his family are there supporting each other, showing their cattle, and if he's not showing his own, his cattle-handling for the Scottsdale High School, or some of the other farming pursuits that they're showing. He's had wins at the Royal Queensland and the Royal Sydney Shows, and that certainly cemented Will's place in the Australian team.

His time in Canada included training and some touring of ranches and purebred operations prior to the competition. The competition concluded on the Sunday evening, and Will was straight on a flight home, landing in Launceston on the Tuesday, in preparation for his hometown show at Scottsdale that weekend. That's how dedicated this young cattle-handler is, not only to what his chosen sport or his interest is, but also to his direct community.

Will described the experience as 'surreal' and something he would cherish forever, and he has potential future plans of spending more time in Canada and introducing Canadian genetics into his own operation of his Heatherfield Angus Stud.

I want to sincerely congratulate Will and the other members of the Australian team on winning B.E.E.F. for Australia. I think it's a wonderful achievement, and again, it just shows what dedication, effort and hard work can be achieved, has been achieved and will continue to be achieved by young Will Hadley.

Members - Hear, hear.

MOTIONS

Alcohol and Drugs Members' Statement

[12.14 p.m.]
Ms FORREST (Murchison) - Mr President, I move the following motion -

With reference to the Interim Report of the Joint Sessional Committee on Workplace Culture Oversight (Paper No. 26), that for the current Parliament, unless otherwise ordered, the Council agree to the following statement for Members in relation to alcohol and other drugs - 

Our acknowledgements 

Attendance at social and community events, official functions, protocol, and other activities where alcohol is served are frequent features of the work of an elected Member of Parliament. We value the reputation of the institution of Parliament, public trust in Members, and the safety and welfare of the people with whom we work. 

We acknowledge that impairment from alcohol or other drugs can significantly increase risks to Work Health and Safety, including during work related travel. 

We acknowledge that alcohol use is correlated with sexual harassment and assault.  

We accept that our role as elected Members of Parliament does not excuse irresponsible consumption of alcohol or being more generally impaired by alcohol and other drugs. 

Our commitments 

We commit to managing the risks associated with alcohol and other drug use and acknowledge our shared responsibility under the Work Health and Safety Act to maintain a safe and respectful workplace for all. 

We commit to principles of responsible consumption of alcohol and preventing the adverse consequences of impairment from alcohol and drug use on staff and other Members in the workplace. 

We respect the choice of an individual to not consume alcohol in any setting where alcohol is served. 

We recognise and respond to inappropriate behaviour, intervening when needed, and encourage and support others to speak up.  [OK]

Mr President, it's taken a while to get here today, but I'm very pleased to be able to move these motions on behalf of this House and the parliament and I know we followed up on it in the other place by the Premier. In doing that, I acknowledge and pay respect to the work of our project team and welcome them to the Chamber. Notably, Andrew Young is the consultant who's been working with the Workplace Culture Oversight committee to deliver these really important parts of the recommendations made. Also Caitlin and Anne, who are no longer with us, but they've done a whole body of work that supports that. They are back in their substantive roles now, but we thank them for being here and acknowledge that enormous amount of work that they have put into this. It's really important that we acknowledge that, Mr President.

I will speak broadly to all three motions before the House. I think it's more effective to do it that way. They are linked in many ways, and I will move the first motion as has been read at the end. 

These three motions are the result of three years of work by the Joint Parliamentary Sessional Committee on Workplace Culture Oversight. The committee first met in September 2022 to oversee the implementation of the recommendations of what is informally known as the Bolt report, that is the report titled Motion for Respect - Report into Workplace Culture in the Tasmanian Ministerial and Parliamentary Services, an independent review of this workplace conducted by the former Anti-Discrimination Commissioner, Sarah Bolt.

I acknowledge the leadership of the Premier and the initial establishment and initial work of the committee. It is important to see the Bolt report in the broader context of the developments in standards in Westminster Parliaments in Australia and overseas. In the four years preceding the Bolt review, similar reviews were conducted in the United Kingdom Parliament, New Zealand, New South Wales, South Australia and perhaps most notably, in the Australian Parliament following the allegations made by Brittany Higgins.

In all cases, codes of conduct have been modernised or established for the first time and, in most cases, independent complaints processes have been set up.

In 2024, the Commonwealth Parliamentary Association published new standards for codes of conduct throughout the Commonwealth. It is broadly accepted that members are part of the workplace, even if their status as employees or employers is unclear.

Members and staff in parliamentary ministerial workplaces have a right to be safe in the workplace. Complaints processes need to be more professional and independent. It is accepted that resources need to be provided to the parliament's Clerks to ensure that training and support for new procedures, including independent complaints processes, are effective.

While this broader context is important, it is worth repeating some key aspects of the Bolt report. The review had a comparatively high survey response rate of 56.5 per cent, including 318 members and staff, an indication that members and staff were highly motivated to speak up. It identified a range of workplace behaviours, in particular bullying, harassment and discrimination. It found that the House's code of conduct was no longer fit for purpose. There were unclear or no avenues for people to make or take complaints. It emphasised the rights of everyone, including members of parliament, to a safe and fair workplace.

The code and the Independent Complaints Commissioner motions have to be read in conjunction to fully understand the scope of what is being proposed. Section 2.1 of the code makes it clear that a complaint may be made against a member, including a minister or presiding officer acting in their capacity as a member.

With regard to former members, section 2.1 of the code also provides that a former member may be held to account for a breach of the code, but only if the alleged breach of the code occurred when they were a member.

With regard to ministers, section 1.1.1 of the Independent Complaints Commissioner motion provides that the commissioner can receive a complaint of alleged behaviour of a minister that breaches section 6 of the code in relation to a safe and respectful workplace. The reason for this specific inclusion is because it would be difficult and counterproductive to decide if a minister accused of bullying or sexually harassing a person had done so in their capacity as a minister or a member. For the avoidance of doubt, the commissioner has no jurisdiction to investigate a complaint that a minister has otherwise breached the Ministerial Code of Conduct. Such matters remain a matter for the Premier and the ministerial code that the Premier oversees.

With regard to people eligible to make a complaint, section 1.7 of the Independent Complaints Commissioner motion provides a description of the people who may make a complaint that a member has breached the code. These people include members of parliament and those employed pursuant to an act of Tasmania or by Crown prerogative, whose duties require them to engage with members of parliament. The prescription means that not only are staff with everyday contact with members such as parliamentary staff, ministerial advisers included, but so is a public servant whose duties may include briefing a member or minister but does not have necessarily have frequent contact with them.

Section 1.8 of the Independent Complaints Commissioner motion provides that a complaint cannot be made if the alleged conduct occurred prior to the resolution of the code of conduct. It further provides that complaints must be logged within two years of the alleged conduct occurring. This timeframe seeks to strike the right balance in acknowledging that a victim of certain behaviour, or a person who knows of improper conduct by a member, may have legitimate reasons for not coming forward immediately. This timeframe is also relevant to the earlier point that if members have since become a former member, the complaint may still be received and acted upon.

Turning to some specific provisions of the code. The Houses have had a code of conduct since 2018. The committee's report has recommended that the Houses resolve an entirely new code that addresses the findings of the Bolt report and reflects the rapidly developing best practice standards for codes of conduct in parliaments, particularly parliaments in the Commonwealth.

 [Bookmark: Phr2_O=25.8_D=29.8_C=0.85] The recommended code of conduct for members is in three parts. Part 1 sets out the principles and scope of the code. It is a preamble to the enforceable standards and includes a range of positive statements which are important in setting a tone that is aspirational and consistent with members being leaders in the community. 

Part 2 of the code is the enforceable standards. That is, only alleged breach of the standard in Part 2 can be the basis for a complaint to the Independent Complaints Commissioner. [Bookmark: Phr3_O=57.4_D=18.8_C=0.81]  Section 3 of Part 2 requires an amendment that each member will publicly affirm in their respective House their commitment to the enforceable standards in the code within the first two sitting weeks of being elected. The code leaves it up to the two Houses to decide the form and mechanism of this affirmation. [Bookmark: Phr4_O=76.7_D=17.5_C=0.81]  It should be noted that in the ACT Legislative Assembly, this is done by way of a motion that all members affirm. In some other parliaments, such as Ontario in Canada, it is done by form of a written pledge. The public affirmation by every member is the best way for members to show leadership to the community.

 [Bookmark: Phr5_O=95.8_D=29.5_C=0.79] Sections 4 and 5 of the code deal with general conduct in public office, including rules regarding engagement of staff, conflicts of interest, lobbying, the use of public resources, financial inducements and complying with the Parliamentary Disclosure of Interest Act 1996. The code does not seek to override or replace rules that are in place, such as the rules for members' use of allowances and resources but requires the members to comply [Bookmark: Phr6_O=125.4_D=1.4_C=0.62]  with those rules.

 [Bookmark: Phr7_O=128.3_D=21.6_C=0.82] The code's requirements that members comply with all the relevant rules, laws and policies regarding the use of public resources found in section 4, therefore requires members to be aware of their entitlements and responsibilities in the Parliamentary Salaries, Superannuation and Allowances Act 2012 combined with the guides produced by Department of Premier and Cabinet and the Legislative Council.

 [Bookmark: Phr8_O=151.4_D=29.4_C=0.79] Section 6 of the code is an important initiative because it lists prohibited workplace behaviours, particularly discrimination, bullying, harassment, sexual harassment and sexual assault and victimisation. The code does not define these behaviours in a unique manner but clearly states that they are to be understood consistently with the relevant laws. This is a key aspect of responding to the Bolt report and acknowledging that members and staff are participants in the contemporary workplace [Bookmark: Phr9_O=181.2_D=9.2_C=0.74]  and that, notwithstanding the cut and thrust of politics, people - members included - have a right to a safe and respectful workplace.

 [Bookmark: Phr10_O=191.9_D=25.2_C=0.82] Section 6 also addresses one of Bolt's key findings about lack of bystander action. Bolt's survey found that only 11 per cent of those who witnessed a prohibited behaviour, such as sexual harassment or bullying, actually intervened. Intervention is not an easy step to take, and the code does not put an unreasonable onus on members. Section 6.2 states that:

 [Bookmark: Phr11_O=217.9_D=3.4_C=0.86] Members shall take appropriate steps to protect the health and safety of their staff [Bookmark: Phr12_O=221.9_D=28.4_C=0.80]  and if they observe or receive a report of unacceptable workplace behaviour toward them. [OK]

This code should be read in conjunction with section 1.5 in the principles of the code, which states:

Members ensure that bullying, harassment, sexual harassment or assault, discrimination or victimisation in any form is not tolerated, condoned or ignored in the parliamentary workplace. Members encourage the reporting of such behaviour and the importance of considering the welfare of those affected. [OK]

 [Bookmark: Phr13_O=251.9_D=29.2_C=0.82] With regard to online behaviour, the prevalence of the use of digital communications and social media means that they have become an extension of the workplace. This is true of many industries where the behaviour of staff online is treated just as seriously as face-to-face behaviour. Section 7 of the code includes the standard that:

Members shall not use social media, messaging services, email or any other online activity in breach of the Commitment to a safe and respectful workplace [Bookmark: Phr14_O=281.6_D=6.6_C=0.82]  section of the code. [OK]

This stand extends to members being accountable for the use of social media by their staff [Bookmark: Phr15_O=289.1_D=8.8_C=0.85]  and, when the member has authorised the staff to act on the member's behalf, a member will not be held accountable if the staff member acts without authorisation.

 [Bookmark: Phr16_O=298.7_D=6.3_C=0.91] Section 7 does not prevent robust debate or opinion being expressed on social media or other digital means, but it does make clear that the use of social media media is not a vehicle for directing behaviour at another member or a person who works with members. That is a breach of a safe and respectful workplace behaviour.

Section 9 of the code provides that all members will participate in induction training in relation to the code within certain timeframes after the adoption of the code and after election to a House. The code does not propose that there will be routine public reporting of attendance, but it may be relevant for a complaint's commissioner investigating allegation against a member to know that if a member attended mandatory training. For this purpose, the code requires the Clerks to maintain a record of attendance.

A natural concern for members in a political environment is that the code and complaints process could be weaponised by making groundless complaints against a member. Section 10 of the enforceable standards requires that a member shall not make a complaint alleging a breach of this code by another member unless they have reasonable grounds to suspect noncompliance, or if such a complaint is frivolous or vexatious. Part 3 of the code provides necessary aspects of governance to ensure that the new system of a code, an independent complaints commissioner will work.

Section 12 provides that induction and training will be provided to members and that the member should ensure that their staff are aware of both the member's obligations and the rights and responsibilities of staff in the workplace. Induction and periodic training are crucial elements to professionalising the role of a member and promoting a positive culture.

Section 13 states an independent complaints process will be established, which is the subject of the third motion, in the order we're doing them. Both sections 12 and 13 make reference to the need for Clerks to be properly resourced and to ensure that training can be provided and that the independent complaints process can be established and continue to operate. The model the committee has put forward to the House is not a large cost model; it's not a bureaucratically over-engineered model. However, it will require that the Clerks be supported with resources to ensure the process succeeds. This is something that was fully considered by the committee and the Premier absolutely gets this and has committed to advocating on behalf of this process.

The other motion relating to the independent complaints commissioner provides for the establishment of an independent complaints commissioner. Until recent years, most parliaments in Westminster jurisdictions relied solely on privileges or standards committees of the House to investigate alleged misconduct by a member. In many cases, Houses and committees construe the role of such committees in very narrow terms, so there was no avenue for complaints to be made about the conduct of a member. Furthermore, privileges committees were not equipped to carry out investigations on the basis of procedural fairness or processes that have regard to the welfare of complainants and members. 

The establishment of the independent complaints commissioner addresses these shortcomings and puts in place an independent complaints mechanism already in place in numerous Australian parliaments, including New South Wales, Victoria, the ACT, the Commonwealth, Canada and the UK.

Section 1 of the motion sets out the functions of the ICC, which includes a resolution of minor complaints and imposition of agreed remedies by the ICC itself. A further function is to carry out more formal investigations of more serious breaches of the code or matters which failed to be conciliated. A function of the ICC will be the provision of information and education on the role of the ICC. It is expected that information education will be provided to both members and staff.

Section 1.1.2 makes it clear that complaints involving proceedings of the Houses or committees, that is those things subject to parliamentary privilege, are not within the scope of the ICC. Similarly, the ICC will not investigate matters in the Criminal Code, or which would constitute serious misconduct in the Integrity Commission Act 2009.

There may, therefore, be a small number of things that could constitute a breach of the code of conduct for members, which the ICC would not investigate because they are more properly a matter for the police or the Integrity Commission. It is expected that people appointed as ICC and Deputy ICC will have the experience and expertise to understand the distinction.

Speaking about the appointment of the ICC and Deputy ICC, section 1.2 requires the Speaker and President, acting jointly, to be joined by a suitably qualified person on a panel to select an ICC and Deputy ICC.  The motion does not include a job description or required qualifications, but the various aspects of the code of the ICC motion inform a recruitment process that would logically appoint a person who understands concepts including procedural fairness, victim welfare and evidence-based finding amongst other things.

This section also requires that there be a gender balance between the ICC and Deputy. This is important given the type and sensitive complaints that could be made, being mindful that victim welfare and preparedness to come forward are sometimes based on whether the person can confide in a man or a woman.

Section 1.4 provides that the appointment panel will consult the Premier, Leader of the Opposition, leaders of other parties, crossbench and the independents prior to appoint the appointment of the ICC and Deputy.
Section 1.5 leaves it to the Presiding Officers to contact the ICC and Deputy on terms and conditions. This will enable Presiding Officers, assisted by the Clerks, to consider a retainer approach for the first instance rather than a full-time engagement.

It is not the intention of this motion to create a bureaucracy and over-engineer the establishment of the ICC. There should be no requirement for staff or separate office accommodation for the ICC in its first iteration. If over time, caseloads increase, and one would hope they wouldn't, there would be opportunities for contract arrangements and the terms of the resolutions to be revisited.

Section 3.3 provides that the ICC may seek advice in relation to the nature of a complaint and jurisdictional issues. This may include the right to seek advice from the Clerks on matters of parliamentary procedure and privilege.

Section 3.5 states that, where possible, the ICC will facilitate early confidential resolution or consideration of a complaint without progressing to a formal process. This is important guidance for the ICC, that some matters are best resolved by bringing the complainant and the member together, or having the ICC conciliate individually with each person in the manner that protects both persons privacy, while also finding a resolution agreeable to both.

Dispute resolution, at this early stage, may include remedies such as a verbal or written apology, correction of the public record, agreement to undertake a training or education session, or other remedies dependent on the act or admission that constitutes the breach of the code.

Section 3.6 requires confidentiality in certain circumstances but also enables the ICC to publicly confirm the fact that a complaint has been received; only in exceptional circumstances where this declaration addresses initial fairness for one or the other person. There may be an exceptional case where public conjecture about a matter means it is fairer to one party or the other that the ICC clarify the fact that a complaint has been made.

Section 4 sets out the processes for investigation of more serious allegations and matters that could not be conciliated.

Section 4.4 provides that the ICC may require a person to attend a meeting and to produce documents. The ICC may report a failure to cooperate to the relevant privileges committee.

Section 4.3 requires the ICC to report findings and recommended sanctions to the relevant privileges committee where the recommended section would require consideration and enforcement by the relevant House. An example is when the ICC recommends that a member make a public apology in the House, or the House resolve to admonish the member.

Section 4.7 enables the privileges committee to receive reports from the ICC and to review only the recommended sanction, not to carry out its own investigation of the allegation. The committee will invite the member who has been found to have breached the code to make a submission only in relation to the recommended sanction. The privileges committee will have to include the ICCs report or a summary of it as part of the report to the House, which provides transparency in relation to the matter in general and in relation to the committee's final recommendation for sanction.

It should be noted that when the Houses have adopted the new code and complaints process, the DPAC handbook and other guides should amend any references to a complaint being made to the Integrity Commission and state that alleged breaches of the members' code of conduct can be directed to the Independent Complaints Commissioner.

To speak specifically to the alcohol and other drugs statement, this motion is a statement rather than a policy to acknowledgements and commitments in relation to alcohol and other drugs. It does not seek to prevent or enforce certain behaviours. Enforceable conduct is a matter dealt with in part 2 of the code of conduct. The statement acknowledges, amongst other things in paragraph three, that members acknowledge the impairment from alcohol and other drugs can significantly increase risk to workplace health and safety, including during work-related travel.

Among the commitment made by members in the statement, members commit to managing the risk associated with alcohol and other drug use and acknowledge that our shared responsibility under Work Health and Safety Act to maintain a safe and respectful workplace for all. While these may appear to be self-evident statements, members in parliament in Australia have not made them as a collective before. We are the first jurisdiction to do this. This not only demonstrates community leadership, it also honours the findings of reviews in several parliamentary workplaces, including Tasmania, that irresponsible consumption of alcohol is often associated with incredibly harmful behaviours such as sexual harassment and assault.

To conclude, I wish to make some observations about the relevance of these matters and some very recent public reporting of issues around parliaments in Australia. In just a few days since the workplace culture committee report was tabled in the House, there have been very public reports of alleged conduct in various parliaments around Australia, all of which support the need for a modern code of conduct and an independent, empowered complaints and investigations process.

In Victoria, during debate on a bill in one of the most recent sitting weeks, a member reflected on being a victim of inappropriate workplace behaviour, including sexual harassment, during her time as both an adviser and an elected member. That member was Georgie Purcell[checked], MLC and the reference is Hansard, Legislative Council, 20 November 2025. These comments attracted media coverage and reflected poorly on the Victorian parliament. In South Australia, media has reported that an independent review of workplace culture in the parliament has found that staff believe politics is prioritised over their welfare and that staff are held to a higher standard than members.

The reference for that is ABC South Australia website, 22 November 2025 by reporter Thomas Kelsall[checked]. This followed a report three years earlier by the Equal Opportunity Commissioner which reported allegations of sexual harassment, indecent exposure and physical assault. A new code was put in place in South Australia, but the most recent report suggests that a code must be accompanied by an independent complaints process, training of members and staff and longer-term cultural change.

Only days earlier, the Federal Court of Appeal was considering an appeal from Senator Hanson[checked], who was found by the Federal Court to have racially discriminated against Senator Faruqi[checked] in a 2022 exchange of tweets. Faruqi versus Hanson, 2004 Federal Court of Australia 1264. This case has cost hundreds of thousands of dollars and has brought to light the way in which social media is often used to convey words that would be a breach of workplace behaviour if spoken face to face.

Words said in a digital form can still be intimidating, harassing, bullying or discriminatory. The decision by Justice Stewart in the 2024 case in favour of Senator Faruqi also highlighted the fact that there are limits to the implied right of freedom of political communication when a member of parliament is saying things on social media that were designed to be personally hurtful. If the Commonwealth parliament had a code of conduct that included online behaviour by members and an independent complaints commissioner that could have conciliated or made findings, Senator Faruqi and Senator Hanson could have had an alternative and a much less costly avenue to resolve the complaint.

This code of conduct proposed by our Tasmanian parliament addresses this issue in a balanced manner. It does not seek to prevent social media being used to conduct robust political debate and opinions, but it does make it clear that the use of social media will not be a vehicle for directing behaviour at another member that is a breach of a safe and respectful workplace.

Mr President, I commend the motions to the House and in this instance, I move-

That the members' Alcohol and Other Drugs Statement be agreed to.

We do work in a different workplace than many other workplaces. We are often here for long hours. We often have meals, sometimes three meals in the same day, in this place. I think that's probably what we're looking at today. We are in a different type of workplace, but we need to remember also that it's not that for everyone in this place, that it is a workplace with a very broad range of roles and experiences.

What I really do support in this statement is that it is about our behaviour. It's not about restricting anyone, but it's about just reminding and making a statement that we acknowledge that our behaviour is important and the way we conduct ourselves in this workplace is important, particularly for those who are around us. The third - well, third in my order, anyway - the motion to establish the independent complaints commissioner and deputy commissioner - which again, I'm very supportive of, but just a couple of comments I really wanted to put on record here.

It is a little different in that the work is not then being led by the government. The preparation hasn't been able to be done. The resourcing hasn't been allocated. There hasn't been that piece of work that we would normally see with a legislated commission or establishment of a commission. So we just need to be mindful of that.

This motion contains timelines. It's putting the responsibility for this onto the presiding officers. We can't hold the presiding officers accountable to that if that resourcing is not provided. I'm interested to hear the response, presumably from the Leader for the Government rather than member for McIntyre, although I'd be very happy to hear both, of course, and when this motion is debated in the other place too, to hear that commitment from the government that they will be committing to adequate resourcing, because if we're going to do this, we need to make sure that it's done well.

I don't think it's as simple as just appointing a complaints commissioner and a deputy because there's obviously infrastructure and resources that need to go around that, and this government does not have a great track record of adequately resourcing accountability bodies. If we look at the Integrity Commission and the Tasmanian Audit Office, those bodies are often talking about how their work is being impacted by lack of resources. If we're going to do this, we need to make sure that it's done well, because people should be able to have confidence that if they do put in a complaint that it is dealt with properly, that there is a robust process and that those complaints will be handled in an appropriate way.

I support all three motions, but in particular that motion with the Independent Complaints Commissioner: we have to get that resourcing right. We have to see that it is unusual. We're in the middle of a budget cycle. The timelines are not going to match up with the next budget, so we will need to see some kind of commitment from the government to make sure that that role is resourced properly, not only in an ongoing way, but in the adequate resourcing to establish it and get it set up well, so that we're not just saying we're going to do this, we're actually going to do it. I will conclude there. I support the motions and I'm happy to see this through the parliament before the end of the year.

[12.52 p.m.]
Ms RATTRAY (McIntyre - Leader for the Government in the Legislative Council) - Mr President, I, like the member for Rumney who's spoken, will combine the contribution from the government into the one as the honourable member for Murchison, the mover of the motion, did, I believe, rather than take three opportunities. I will do my best to cut out what I don't need as I make my way forward.

I rise to address the motions, the first one being the alcohol and drugs member statement, and acknowledge that the motion reflects an important recommendation from the Motion for Respect Report and Workplace Culture Oversight Committee's findings. Before I move on to the key points, I also want to place on the record, not only on behalf of the government but as the member for McIntyre, my acknowledgement of the work that has been put in by the committee. It is particularly nice to see the panel members here as well today and to see the next part of the work that they have provided to the parliament, acknowledging the work and I endorse the comments made by the member for Rumney. I couldn't have said it any better myself, so why would I?

The Motion for Respect Report identified alcohol as a contributing factor for harassment and unsafe behaviour in parliamentary settings. This motion acknowledges that risk, links it to work health and safety obligations and commits members to the responsible consumption of alcohol and bystander intervention. The Workplace Culture Oversight Committee second interim report reinforced the need for cultural expectations around responsible consumption, and acknowledged a first step for members is recognising the risk associated with alcohol in the workplace. 

I believe this statement delivers those expectations. It certainly sets out clear acknowledgements: impairment increases work health and safety risks, alcohol correlates with harassment and members' roles do not excuse irresponsible behaviour. It commits members to managing risks, respecting non-drinkers and intervening where inappropriate behaviours occur. While this is a positive cultural signal, it's noted it is not enforceable. The Motion for Respect Report recommended stronger policy levers and accountability measure, and while this motion does not create sanctions, it does articulate a set of standards for acceptable behaviour, which is certainly a positive move in the right direction. The statement is a practical measure setting the standard of behaviour expected by members.

This statement progresses the cultural intent of the review by embedding shared responsibility and prevention principles. It is a step forward in shaping a respectful parliamentary environment.

In regard to the code of conduct for members of the Legislative Council, recognising the development of this is the cornerstone of cultural reform and it responds directly to the motion of respect report. Again, the workplace culture oversight committees call for enforceable standards.

 The motion of respect report recommended a clear, enforceable code of conduct for members to ensure a safe and respectful environment for all staff. This motion delivers that through principles and enforceable standards. The code establishes:

Respect for the rule of law, the separation of powers and ethical leadership;
Recognition of Tasmania's Aboriginal peoples and their enduring connection to Country;
A commitment to safety, particularly for children and vulnerable people;
Zero tolerance for bullying, harassment, discrimination and victimisation; 
Clear expectations around conflicts of interest, lobbying and the use of public resources;
Mandatory induction and training for all members;
An independent complaints process with proportionate and evidence-based outcomes. 

The code also provides for regular review and the publication of interpretive guidelines to support members in meeting their obligations. The Workplace Culture Oversight Committee emphasised induction, review and independent oversight, which is noted and reflected in this code.

Part 1 of the proposed code sets principles: respect, integrity, cultural recognition and safety. 

Part 2 introduces enforceable standards in workplace behaviour, conflicts of interest, lobbying, online conduct including AI, confidentiality and mandatory induction. 

Part 3 establishes governance, ethics advice, training, complaints processes and review.

A clear strength in the proposed code is that it embeds work, health and safety duties and explicitly prohibits bullying, harassment and discrimination. These are core recommendations in the motion of respect report, and it's pleasing to see that the code seeks to address emerging risks such as online behaviour and AI use. The code reflects the intent of the work and the recommendations of the motion of respect report and lays the foundation for accountability and cultural change.

In regard to the Independent Complaints Commissioner, which was the third part of those motions, the motion for respect report calls for strengthening of the currently limited mechanisms for responding to complaints regarding the conduct of parliamentarians, and places a strong emphasis on independence, procedural fairness.

The Council suspended from 1 p.m. to 2.30 p.m.
