MOTION

Go Gentle Australia Voluntary Assisted Dying in Residential Aged Care Homes – A National Report Card - Consideration and Noting
[5.18 p.m.]

Mr GAFFNEY (Mersey) - Mr President, I move - 

That Go Gentle Australia Voluntary Assisted Dying in Residential Aged Care Homes – A National Report Card, be considered and noted.

I take again the opportunity to rise to bring to the attention in this place the Go Gentle Australia Voluntary Assisted Dying in Residential Aged Care Homes – A National Report Card. It's not a very long contribution, but important, especially with our own review imminent. I have on good authority from the health minister that the panel is nearly finalised. Once that's finalised, the terms of reference for the three-year review as required by our legislation will be advertised for people from across Tasmania and across the mainland to have input into that.

I have previously forwarded to all members a copy of the Assisted Dying in Residential Age Care Reforms, and it's not a huge report, but it's one that has some very good information for us to improve upon across not only in this state, but across the nation.

When we speak of aged care, we are speaking of the homes of people in their last chapters of their lives. For a person entering an aged care facility, that will be their final place they can call home and also often with only a few of the keepsakes and photographs to hopefully remember a life well lived. When we think of a home we are speaking of dignity, choice and true autonomy. A place of your own.

For a person with a terminal condition that may be enduring unbearable suffering, that sense of control can make all the difference. In Australia, more than 8000 people have applied for access to voluntary assisted dying since 2019, when it first became a legal option in Victoria. All states then report an increase in applications over time with the median age of 73 of those applying for VAD, the overwhelming majority dying of cancer, with 80 per cent that have access to palliative care, all to ease their intolerable suffering from a terminal condition. That's why the right to access to voluntary assisted dying in the place you live must be an inalienable human right.

 [Bookmark: Phr2_O=26.0_D=3.2_C=0.58] The findings in the National Report on Voluntary Assisted Dying [Bookmark: Phr3_O=29.6_D=20.6_C=0.75]  in Residential Aged Care Homes demands our attention, not as an abstract critique of the sector, but as a challenge to the conscience of us all. The report sought to investigate what information was made available to residents and potential residents of aged care facilities about VAD as a potential end of life choice. [Bookmark: Phr4_O=53.2_D=5.5_C=0.81]  It also looked at the level of access to VAD that an aged care home might allow for resident.

 [Bookmark: Phr5_O=59.6_D=29.4_C=0.82] It tells us that whilst voluntary assisted dying is now a lawful choice in every state and the ACT commencing on 1 November this year, far too many residential aged care homes are not giving prospective and current residents clear, accessible information about whether or even how that choice can be exercised in what is their final home. Both facts sit uneasily together. The law recognises the right; the law too often erects an invisible barrier.

 [Bookmark: Phr7_O=92.5_D=1.0_C=0.86] Let me be frank. [Bookmark: Phr8_O=94.2_D=29.9_C=0.80]  When a person moves into a residential aged care home, that facility is their home in every meaningful way. It should not be a place where vital choices are hidden or withheld. To enter a home that often comes at considerable expense without knowing whether the end-of-life options you lawfully hold will be respected is a grave inequity, boarding on malfeasance.

The 25 largest national aged care providers and 20 largest service providers in each state [Bookmark: Phr9_O=124.6_D=8.4_C=0.75]  were contacted directly as this is a national report, given the chance to respond and encouraged be transparent in their response. [Bookmark: Phr10_O=134.0_D=26.1_C=0.75]  The result was a traffic light style rating assessment. Some providers with clear public and person-centred information and full access to the entire VAD process. Others with limited information and only secondary access to VAD processes from external VAD health professionals, or a surprisingly large number with no public information on VAD access or that it restricts or does not support VAD on its premises.

 [Bookmark: Phr11_O=160.8_D=13.2_C=0.79] The purpose of these inquiries is not to castigate, but to shed light so that families can make informed choices, providers can understand where best practise lies, and governments can ensure that law and care align.

 [Bookmark: Phr12_O=174.7_D=16.3_C=0.76] The Aged Care Quality and Safety Commission in a 2024 report on aged care stated - [tbc 5.23]

A provider that decides not to take part will still have to meet its provider responsibility to support residents right to exercise choice to access the VAD process.

Why does this matter? [Bookmark: Phr13_O=192.0_D=29.6_C=0.82]  Because restricting access to information about voluntary assisted dying causes harm. If a person is forced to move in the final weeks or days of their life to exercise a lawful choice, the harm is real. It is physical, it is emotional, it is often vindictive. Short journeys can be agonising. Transfers away from familiar environments can diminish the quality of dying. We must not create a system in which only those with the means or the capacity to uproot [Bookmark: Phr14_O=221.8_D=24.2_C=0.69]  themselves can truly access their legal options. There is also an argument about dignity. Person-centred care is not a generic slogan. It's a standard that must be reflected in daily practise. Respecting a resident's right to be informed about their options, including VAD where it is lawful, is an essential part of respecting them as members of our community with their own values, beliefs and choices that must be honoured.

 [Bookmark: Phr15_O=247.3_D=3.4_C=0.70] The report gave a state-by-state breakdown [Bookmark: Phr16_O=251.8_D=6.8_C=0.81] . I'm happy to report that whilst almost all states could do better, Tasmania did better than most [Bookmark: Phr17_O=259.2_D=12.9_C=0.72] , with a nation leading 50 per cent of Tasmanian providers making comprehensive VAD information available despite no obligation to do so, this is against a national average of 18 per cent. [Bookmark: Phr18_O=272.8_D=27.6_C=0.73]  As an aside, our legislation is silent on residential aged care and VAD access where it's a right in South Australia, Queensland, NSW legislation for permanent residents. Tasmania at 30 per cent also has the highest proportion of providers with public information confirming full access to VAD and 20 per cent with secondary access to VAD. It still leaves 50 per cent with no restricted or unpublic [Bookmark: Phr19_O=301.5_D=3.0_C=0.47]  access which, whilst low by the national average of an 85 per cent, is still half of our providers that choose not to share that information. To its credit, One Care Tasmania and particularly the team at One Care Rubicon Grove in Port Sorrell, which I'm pleased is in my patch, was highlighted in the report as an example of best-practice to other providers.

The report gave an example of residents of VAD Journey with staff that were well informed, compassionate and accommodating and went beyond and above the care for his needs as he went through the VAD process. It's examples like these that show what is possible in a faith-based setting where it's the need of a person that comes first and foremost.

As an aside, one of the nurses that was involved with that - and she won't mind me saying - involved with that VAD event, actually we did a 20-kilometre horse ride and she spoke about what happened and how wonderful it was and how the family were so thankful and how the whole Rubicon Grove, they knew what was going on, everyone was so respectful of the process.

It actually was a good learning experience not only for the staff but other residents and how it could be done properly. Glenn Williams, the Chief Clinical Officer at One Care, has a view that their aged-care homes play a critical role in creating a calm, supportive environment and ensuring continuity of care.

They've worked closely with the state's VAD Commission and the VAD Care Navigator Team to educate staff and foster ongoing communication with residents. He went on to say:

It shouldn't be a clinical event; it's part of life. We provide a natural home-like environment for residents who choose VAD.

The report sets out four clear recommendations and they bear repeating here because they are straightforward and they are practical. First, providers must make public, clear and up-to-date information about voluntary assisted dying. That information must be written in clear language that's free of jargon, free of moralising strictures, and easy to find.

If a right exists in law, access to knowledge of that right should not be hidden away in hearsay or the owner's policy doctrine. A person having to decide between homes needs to know upfront: does this place allow voluntarist to die on-site, under what conditions? What supports will be provided and who can assist me in understanding the process?

Secondly, providers should give a clear public explanation of the level of VAD access supported on their premises and the stages of the VAD process can happen here. Some providers may choose not to actively participate in the process. That is their legal perspective and conscientious objection is a recognised stance, no matter how moralistically cruel it is, but that position carries responsibility.

A provider who will not participate must still support a resident's legal right to access the process. They must explain how they will facilitate it, whether by permitting external practitioners on site, by offering temporary relocation within the facility or by assisting with transport arrangements and the limits of those arrangements.

Thirdly, providers need a proper voluntary assisted dying policy, procedures that staff understand, trainings that build competence and confidence, and practical steps to ensure residents' wishes are respected without causing unnecessary delay or distress.

We need a culture where end-of-life conversations are handled with skill and compassion and within the obligations of the legislation. I want to put on the record that that's where our legislation is in advance to some of the others in the fact that we do allow our registered nurses to do the trainings, do the modules, and some of those registered nurses do take up positions within aged-care facilities and that's a very big bonus for the Tasmanian situation.

Lastly, state and federal governments should monitor and encourage residential aged-care providers to comply fully with relevant legislation, regulatory framework and obligations. I want to emphasise that these recommendations are not merely bureaucratic; they are moral and practical. They preserve choice, reduce harm and ensure equity. Where regional and rural communities are faced with limited alternatives, these measures are especially important. The ability to find another facility 100 kilometres away is not an option for someone in their last days.

Dr Linda Swan, as the CEO of Go Gentle Australia, has heard of frail and sick aged-care residents being forced to move to another care home to access VAD - not here in our state. She has also heard from residents who have only learned about VAD when it was too late, as well as families who are angry that their loved ones suffered unnecessarily. She said:

Older people have a legal right to know their options at the end of life, including VAD, and they have a right to know this information before they choose the facility that they will become their home. There is a further pragmatic point. Good transparency builds trust. Providers that are open about their position and their processes will gain the confidences of prospective residents and their families. The report shares examples of best-practice providers. They show that clarity is possible and that it can be achieved with compassion for residents' immediate needs and without moral compromise.  [Bookmark: Phr2_O=30.2_D=30.0_C=0.65] Even in faith-based organisation, maybe as a perfect expression of the Christian faith where kindness and compassion are a guiding tenet.

In closing I return to a simple principle where your home should not be a place where legal choices are concealed. A person's final residence should be where their dignity is honoured and their autonomy is respected and fully supported. This report is a call to action. We have Tasmania's example of best practice that demonstrates  [Bookmark: Phr3_O=60.3_D=7.6_C=0.82] clarity from providers with practical policies and training that allows staff to support residents with kindness and compassion.

 [Bookmark: Phr4_O=69.0_D=23.3_C=0.84] Above all, let us make sure that when the law grants the choice to a person on how they might end their intolerable suffering, the reality of care does not take it away from them. 

I would like to also acknowledge the Minister for Health, Mental Health and Wellbeing in the Chamber today, and I would like to put on the record my gratitude for her recent announcement that there will be a portal for  [Bookmark: Phr5_O=93.4_D=2.5_C=0.54] voluntary assisted dying I have had many  [Bookmark: Phr6_O=97.9_D=0.2_C=0.96] doctors  [Bookmark: Phr7_O=98.5_D=7.2_C=0.81] contact me and saying that is a great step, so I do acknowledge and thank you for that. 

I can remember in my journey when I travelled to  [Bookmark: Phr8_O=107.6_D=21.7_C=0.73] Vancouver early on. I met with Dr Ellen Wiebe who was the main instigator of the medicinal assistance in dying in Canada, her and [inaudible] who wrote the legislation. They had a specific issue in Vancouver where there was a faith-based hospice that was against, and it happened  [Bookmark: Phr9_O=129.6_D=30.0_C=0.81] that they were forced to leave that place because of the guidelines associated with that hospice. But eventually they took it to their High Court, I am not going to say the Supreme Court, their highest court in the land and they challenged that, saying that the person's rights outweighed the rights of the guidelines of the hospice. Therefore, that was allowed to take place because she said it was far too [Bookmark: Phr10_O=160.0_D=14.8_C=0.90]  harmful for the person to be moved in the last few days of their life, in such pain, and that would be inhumane. On those things, I appreciate  [Bookmark: Phr11_O=176.2_D=15.2_C=0.77] the aged care homes from Go Gentle. I congratulate them on all they work to do, and I am really hoping that the Northern Territory can join with the other states and territories in Australia and be able to offer  [Bookmark: Phr12_O=192.2_D=4.6_C=0.80] those people suffering the choice of voluntary assisted dying. I commend the report.
 [Bookmark: Phr13_O=197.9_D=6.0_C=0.76] 
[5.33 p.m.]
 [Bookmark: Phr14_O=211.5_D=27.6_C=0.82] Ms RATTRAY (McIntyre - Leader for the Government in the Legislative Council) - Mr President, thank you, and I also thank the honourable member for Mersey for bringing this important report card to the council's attention. Also today, I would like to take this opportunity to again thank the honourable member for his continued commitment, passion and work over many years on the End of Life Choices Voluntary Assisted Dying Bill and your continued advocacy in this space.

 [Bookmark: Phr15_O=240.2_D=29.3_C=0.85] The government absolutely supports the aims of the Go Gentle Australia Voluntary Assisted Dying in the Residential Aged-Care Home National Report Card. It is fundamental that aged care residents have the right to access services which are legally available to other people in the community, along with the information about those services needed, to support informed decision-making for themselves and for their families.
 [Bookmark: Phr16_O=269.8_D=2.4_C=0.68]  [Bookmark: Phr17_O=272.9_D=6.5_C=0.86] The report card notes a general lack of transparency around access [Bookmark: Phr18_O=280.1_D=17.1_C=0.80]  to voluntary assisted dying for residents in residential aged-care facilities, demonstrating that many residential aged-care facilities are not providing residents the minimal required information about the voluntary assisted dying service.

 [Bookmark: Phr19_O=297.8_D=7.2_C=0.83] It should be noted that the comments by Go Gentle are, of course, broadly presented and paint a picture of the practices across all Australian jurisdictions where voluntary assisted dying is legal. It is worth pointing out that the statistics presented by Go Gentle, when broken down by each jurisdiction, show that Tasmanian aged care facilities score better and higher than aged care facilities in any other jurisdictions.

According to the report card, it is pleasing to note that Tasmanian aged care facilities provide better quality of voluntary assisted dying information than aged care facilities in other jurisdictions. A better proportion of Tasmanian aged care facilities provide full access to voluntary assisted dying than in any other jurisdictions. Compared with other jurisdictions, Tasmanian aged care facilities are less likely to provide no access or restricted access to voluntary assisted dying services.

With voluntary assisted dying legislated in Tasmania in late 2022, and there will be some here that will remember that well, it's commendable Tasmanian aged care facilities have quickly accommodated their communications and practices to this statewide service which is available to all eligible Tasmanians. It has encouraged those aged care facilities who are not communicating about this statewide service to consider reaching out to the commission to seek further information on the documents and support available. 

In supporting these facilities to develop policies for voluntary assisted dying in Tasmania, the government makes available to every facility a guideline document on policy development and provides also ongoing policy advice, education and, as needed, updated information on the legislative requirements of voluntary assisted dying. The Tasmanian Voluntary Assisted Dying Navigation Service provides more generally a central point of contact for information and support about voluntary assisted dying to people, families, carers and health professionals, including residential aged care residents and their families, and health workers and administrators of residential aged care facilities.

With the ongoing help and guidance provided by the government, and with the support of the Tasmanian Statewide Voluntary Assisted Dying Navigation Service, it is expected that the residents of Tasmanian aged care facilities will continue to be supported in making an informed choice. This support, and the information and care which underpins it, is the right of all residents of aged care facilities, no matter whether a resident considers voluntary assisted dying to be a suitable option for themselves or not. Mr. President, the motion is supported.

MOTION

Project Marinus Whole-of-State Business Case and Federal Government Agreement

Mr GAFFNEY (Mersey) - Mr President, I move - 

That the Legislative Council calls on the government to release immediately:
the full, unredacted Project Marinus Whole-of-State Business Case; and

the Project Marinus agreement signed by Minister Duigan and the federal government.

In the lead-up to the final investment decision on Project Marinus, the government made a promise to the Tasmanian people. It pledged to provide a whole-of-state business case which was to comprehensively consider the opportunities and impact of Project Marinus for Tasmania, and pledged to do so 30 days in advance of making a final investment decision. The government received the finalised business case from the Department of Treasury and Finance on 16 May 2025 and yet released nothing to the public for a long time afterwards. Just over a fortnight later, the no-confidence motion passed the other place, and we were in full-on election mode, barely 16 months after the last one. We all know how that turned out.

 [Bookmark: Phr2_O=27.4_D=24.5_C=0.78] In normal circumstances, you would hope the salutary and sobering response for the government may have caused a pause for thought. Back to Marinus, with pre-and-post-election chatter about the yet-to-be-published business case, the government was very shy about sharing the details with elected members. Opposition and crossbench members of parliaments, after signing confidentiality agreements to prevent any public discussion [Bookmark: Phr3_O=52.4_D=2.5_C=0.63] , were finally given limited access to the documents [Bookmark: Phr4_O=55.5_D=4.3_C=0.83] , frustratingly only for a confidential, read-only briefing [Bookmark: Phr5_O=62.1_D=24.1_C=0.72]  on 29 July, after which minister Nick Duigan, as Minister for Energy and Renewables, immediately signed the Marinus Agreement with the federal government in the post-election caretaker period. 

I'm sure we can all remember that this was a particularly fractious period, with the Premier still lacking the certainty that his minority government would continue [Bookmark: Phr6_O=86.6_D=5.4_C=0.83] , a matter that was finally put to rest on 19 August with Labor's failed no-confidence motion. [Bookmark: Phr7_O=92.5_D=29.8_C=0.79]  It was only after the agreement was signed and locked in did the government at last provide the whole-of-state business case to the public, breaking the promise made to Tasmanians to release the document 30 days before making the final investment decision. What is more, significant portions of the business case were redacted. On its website, the Department of Treasury and Finance states that:

The full report provides substantial additional information, but has sections which have been redacted to protect confidential and commercially-sensitive information [Bookmark: Phr8_O=123.0_D=11.1_C=0.65]  at the request of the following affected parties: TasNetworks, Marinus Link Pty Ltd, Hydro Tasmania, Clean Energy Finance Corporation and the Australian Government.

 [Bookmark: Phr9_O=134.7_D=27.6_C=0.77] The extent to which the Marinus business case was redacted was surprising to many. If it was to be printed, you would use a fair bit of black ink and toner. Tasmanians had expected to receive considerably more information than they were allowed to read. The result was a strong public backlash to the government's handling of the Marinus business case and final investment decision. In news articles, press releases and public meetings, the wider community expressed outrage at what had taken place.

 [Bookmark: Phr10_O=163.5_D=29.4_C=0.73] 'Public trust in the state's roll-out of renewables, particularly in areas most impacted by Marinus, is on a knife edge,' said Environment Tasmania CEO James Overington in a press release. 'Pushing this enormous and highly controversial project through behind closed doors and whilst in caretaker mode would represent a killer blow to any hopes of securing social licence for Tasmanian's energy transition,' energy and climate campaigner Maddie McShane added.

'The level of [Bookmark: Phr11_O=193.0_D=25.0_C=0.74]  secrecy regarding this project is undemocratic,' Bob Campbell, chair of the Tomahawk Protection Society, wrote in a press release. 'What is in the business case that the Liberals want to hide from everyone? This is becoming a pattern of behaviour: get an expert report and then ignore it if it does not say what you want to hear. Tasmanians deserve more from their elected representatives.' Kim Phillips-Haines, a tourism [Bookmark: Phr12_O=218.7_D=2.8_C=0.42]  business owner and representative of Tas Power Democracy [Bookmark: Phr13_O=221.9_D=3.8_C=0.79] , who spoke at a community meeting in Ulverstone, had the following to say [Bookmark: Phr14_O=226.7_D=29.4_C=0.76] :

For those of you who have not yet looked at the Marinus business case document, I encourage you to do so. It is deeply troubling. In places the document is heavily redacted, with entire pages almost completely blackened out and whole graphs covered with black or else their numbers concealed.

The strength of public feeling that erupted in relation to the redacted Marinus business case led 12 Tasmanian community groups and organisations, including the Bob Brown Foundation [Bookmark: Phr15_O=256.2_D=9.6_C=0.71]  and the Tasmanian Constitution Society, to write an open letter to the Energy minister, Nick Duigan, calling him either to restore democracy to energy policy or else resign. [Bookmark: Phr16_O=266.4_D=13.7_C=0.75]  The open letter raised further issues about the terms of the Marinus Link Agreement signed by minister Duigan, particularly in relation to whether the contract has a termination clause as specified in the guidelines on caretaker conventions.  [Bookmark: Phr17_O=280.6_D=18.2_C=0.79] The 12 Tasmanian organisations expressed their concerns that if the Marinus Agreement does not contain the required termination clause, I quote from the letter:

It could set a dangerous precedent which could potentially be used by future governments to justify flagrant breaches of caretaker conventions.

 [Bookmark: Phr18_O=300.4_D=4.6_C=0.55] Craig Garland MP posed a question on behalf of a constituent to Mr Duigan in the other place on 11 September 2025, requesting to know whether the Marinus agreement contained the specified termination clause. However, in the Energy Minister's nearly two-page reply to this question on 17 October, there was no clear answer to Mr Garland's question. Mr President, perhaps the minister would like to help clarify his response with us here today.

Another issue raised since then is whether all the proper processes were followed when Mr Duigan, as Minister for Energy and Renewables, signed a new agreement to advance the Marinus Link project with the Federal Government raising Tasmania's 2022 commitment to stage one from $53 million to $103 million and what effect potential errors might have on the validity of the Marinus contract.

Legal advice from council has been obtained which confirms this concern. If that advice is correct, then the validity of the agreement could be constitutionally compromised. It leaves open the possibility the State of Tasmania may not be legally bound under the agreement and that obligations purportedly entered into could be unenforceable. This is not a mere technicality. It goes to the heart of constitutional compliance in the integrity of government decision making. I quote excerpts from that legal advice, [tbc 5.46]

On the 31st of July 2025, Duigan declared that he personally signed a new agreement with the Commonwealth Government to advance the Marinus Link project. No publicly available copy of the agreement Nick Duigan signed on the 31st of July 2025 has been disclosed or released. News reports confirm the deal was struck and announced that the Tasmanian Government has only issued press releases and media statements summarising its terms, not the full contract text.

The quote continues.

The new agreement, signed by Nick Duigan as the current Minister for Energy and Renewals on the 31st of July 2025, purportedly raises Tasmanian's financial commitment to $103 million, marking the final investment decision for Stage 1. There is no indication as to where the Duigan signed merely a political commitment or he signed a binding agreement contract committing Tasmania to legally binding financial obligations with another party or parties. This signing occurred during the caretaker period without tabling of the business case and without evidence of statutory delegation or governor's authority. If he signed a binding agreement in his own name or ministerial title, it could be said that Duigan acted outside the scope of lawful power and in direct contradiction to administrative frameworks. This could place Duigan in having signed major public funding agreements without lawful authority in defiance of statutory and constitutional requirements. To assist in determining Tasmania's liability and whether the Marinus Agreement legally binds Tasmania, it would be prudent to obtain copies of the agreement Duigan signed. Hence it remains uncertain if Tasmania is legally bound or whether it is party to a non-binding political commitment only.

End of quote. Mr President, what is certain is that the whole state business case has not been tabled and no enabling legislation has passed this place prior to the signing of the agreement, notwithstanding the potential breach of the constitution and lawful powers. The minister signed the agreement barely 10 days after the election.

Outwardly, it appears to be a rash action with a degree of hast and impropriety, especially so when the government and its public service were still subject to caretaker conventions. Even more so when the ability for the Liberal Party to even form a new government had not yet been fully established. The controversy surrounding the Marinus whole state business case and the agreement signed with the federal government has severely undermined public trust in the entire project.

 One of the consequences of that proportion of landholders affected by the North West Transmission Developments have refused to sign any contract or accept any offer of strategic benefit payment or accept any other compensation and say they will not permit the construction of transmission infrastructure on their land. They issued a joint statement to that effect, declaring that the proposed North West Transmission Developments and Marinus Development is not in Tasmania's best interests and does not have a social licence.

These landholders have further told the media that they intend to take legal action against TasNetworks, which could make it difficult for the North West Transmission Developments project to be delivered on time and on budget, even if it eventually ends up going ahead. For a Liberal government to lose the trust of landowners is surely a first and points to the frustration and distrust in the process that has been created by the actions of this government - actions that appear to have violated caretaker conventions and concealed the issue from genuine and fully informed public debate.

As a third Liberal government in as many years, with two of them as minorities, I would have thought it would have been keenly aware of the Tasmanian community's exasperation with its dismal track record in managing major projects. Yet it merrily continues on its way, committing future generations to projects that defy independent expert analysis and load us with insurmountable debt.

Whilst individual members of the government may have financial security and wealth beyond the imagination of a community struggling with the cost-of-living crisis, Tasmanians are keenly aware of the risks to our home state of overextending government borrowing on what appears to be vanity projects.

The rating agency Moody's has just downgraded our state credit rating to a AA3. In their words, this was due to a: [tbc 5.51]

sharp, sustained increase in Tasmania's debt and interest burdens…

and:

although large-scale investments in energy, ports and Australian projects may support long-term growth and revenue, their substantial upfront costs will add to debt burden, weaken debt affordability and contribute to pressure on recurring operating expenses.

What this credit rating downgrade means in practical terms is that the government will have to pay higher interest rates on new debt and when rolling over maturing debt. According to renowned economist Saul Eslake, every dollar that the government spends on interest is a dollar that can't be spent on hospitals, schools, police, policing and other services. We could be in for even more pain in the future, because as Mr Eslake explains in his independent review of Tasmania's state finances, at the rate our government debt is climbing, there could be yet another downgrade to Tasmania's credit rating within the next 10 years.

If the government is to have any hope of regaining public trust and obtaining the necessary social licence for the Marinus Link and the North West Transmission developments, it must release the full unredacted Marinus business case as well as the Marinus agreement that Energy minister Nick Duigan sighed with the federal government.

If the project is too good to turn down, then what does the government have to hide? Show us the detail. Only then will the people of Tasmania have an opportunity to properly examine the information and decide whether these projects truly stack up and are in the best interest of our state.

We cannot afford to fly blind into these projects. Marinus is the largest proposed investment in Tasmania's history, and the people have a right to know what they're being signed up for. To bulldoze these projects through to the construction phase amidst ongoing public controversy and claims of government secrecy will only further erode public trust and would go against expected standards of open democracy.

We are not in the USA where apparently what the President says goes and is completely unaccountable. The Tasmanian government must release the unredacted business case. It must release the Marinus agreement signed by the minister. Only then can we have a sense of true moral commitment to transparent democracy. I thank members for their attention.

I commend the motion to the House.

[5.53 p.m.]
Mr DUIGAN (Windermere - Minister for Energy and Renewables) - Mr President, it is my pleasure to rise on this occasion to speak to the motion and outline the final investment decision on Project Marinus and the material that underpinned it. First of all, I would like to emphasise that the government has always sought to be open and transparent in its decisions on Project Marinus. There was, and remains, a great deal of governance oversight around this project. As the member has mentioned, it is a very, very large project. It is an important project for Tasmania's future. It is a project that exists in two main parts: Marinus Link, which is the undersea part, and the Northwest Transmission Development, the Burnie to Cressy coastal route, which is stage 1 of Marinus Link.

What I have said as minister, and what the government has said all along, is we would only make a positive final investment decision on Project Marinus if it stacked up for Tasmania. I am absolutely confident that Project Marinus will deliver long-term economic and energy security benefits for Tasmania. It will of course support new investment in renewable energy generation. That's one thing it will help. It will stabilise the national electricity market with massive carbon offset benefits as coal is exited from that market and ensure Tasmania can maximise the value of its hydroelectric resources and by doing so allow Tasmanians to enjoy more returns from the outstanding assets that our forefathers and forebears are constructed on the island throughout the course of the last 100 years.

The project is also expected to generate billions in additional economic activity and support thousands of jobs, and Marinus Link will unlock new opportunities for advanced manufacturing support emerging industries again, carbon reducing industries like clean fuels and hydrogen derivatives. Of course, there are other opportunities as well in that space, in the load generating area.

The final investment decision needed to be made under caretaker conventions was neither desirable or avoidable and that's the very clear case that we as government were put in. We were very clear prior to being aware an election would be called that Tasmania's final investment decision, understanding that Project Marinus is a tripartite entity owned by the Tasmanian, Commonwealth and Victorian Governments and there were and remained very hard deadlines around procurement orders that Marinus Link had for both its cable and its converter stations.

That was not a deadline that we imposed. It was a very real deadline and once the election was called the government, of course, operated under the caretaker convention. An important part of the governance put in place to manage the assessment of project Marinus in the lead up to the FID was the work overseen by the Energy Executive Steering Committee, which is a secretary level steering committee led by and chaired by Secretary Limpkin.

From the perspective of the government, we're aware that caretaker convention states a preference for major policy decisions to be avoided if possible, so as not to unnecessarily buy into future government. What needs to be recognised in this instance and as we tried to move that final investment decision date beyond the election and hopefully into a state where a a new government could make that decision.

Eventually we became against a rock and a hard place and had to make a decision, and this is important, noting that no decision would have a very material impact on the project. There was not an opportunity to not make a decision by doing nothing, we risked adding five years potentially to the project and that was very strong, very consistent, unambiguous advice coming from numerous sources.

The positive FID announced on 1 August, and the positive FID was consistent with advice received on the Project Marinus FID assessment report and that was signed off by the Secretary level Energy Executive Steering committee. We also had an expert advisory panel which similarly gave positive advice to government.

Likewise, we received positive advice in in the nature of positive FID recommendations from both Marinus Link and from TasNetworks. There was a lot of information coming in and feeding this decision. At the same time, and it's important to recognise there were ongoing discussions with the commonwealth government about what it might be able to do to make Marinus Link even better for Tasmanians, we were - and I was - engaged heavily in those discussions [Bookmark: Phr2_O=13.2_D=16.5_C=0.84] . In terms of information contained within the whole-of-state business case, we were very reluctant and remain very reluctant to share some of that information about, particularly from my perspective, [Bookmark: Phr3_O=30.8_D=0.6_C=0.90]  [Bookmark: Phr4_O=33.2_D=10.3_C=0.91]  the information that relates to our state-owned electricity generator. The commonwealth would potentially seek to have the state [Bookmark: Phr5_O=45.1_D=23.7_C=0.80]  use that entity to provide certain outcomes and we were very keen information pertaining to that valuable state-held entity remains confidential.

Having said that through the caretaker conventions [Bookmark: Phr6_O=69.9_D=1.0_C=0.80] , which would [Bookmark: Phr7_O=72.5_D=14.3_C=0.81]  suggest or call for a government who was making a decision to share that information and the nature of that decision with the opposition, Well, we went further than that and shared our thinking and the information behind it [Bookmark: Phr8_O=87.0_D=16.2_C=0.88]  with all parliamentarians, where you were given access to all of the documentation that had informed our decision, noting it was only for a brief period of time. Since that time, I have offered to make that unredacted [Bookmark: Phr9_O=103.6_D=26.2_C=0.84]  information available to the Public Accounts Committee. I have written, and the Treasurer has written to the chair of the Public Accounts Committee offering that documentation to live within PAC, noting that PAC has certain legislation surrounding it that would protect that information, which we would seek to continue to protect.

 [Bookmark: Phr12_O=150.7_D=27.0_C=0.81] The FID decision documents which is the FID assessment report, the expert advisory panel report and the whole estate business case which have been released publicly, but in a redacted form. There were commercial and other confidential information contained within those documents and none of the redactions were made by [Bookmark: Phr13_O=177.9_D=5.4_C=0.80]  me or any other ministers. They were made by departments, as you would expect [Bookmark: Phr14_O=186.0_D=6.3_C=0.88] . The key conclusions and recommendations, importantly in those documents, have not been redacted. [Bookmark: Phr15_O=195.4_D=12.0_C=0.67]  As I was saying, with Public Accounts, the Public Accounts Committee Act 1970 provide protections for confidential material. The commonwealth, as is part of our agreement [Bookmark: Phr16_O=210.3_D=10.0_C=0.89] , the agreement between the three parties, agreed to release of the unredacted material to the Public Accounts Committee on the condition of that confidentiality.

 [Bookmark: Phr17_O=222.2_D=9.8_C=0.75] I would say that offer to release to PAC was not accepted, but the offer remains. However, I would take this opportunity [Bookmark: Phr18_O=233.9_D=4.1_C=0.88] , because I think the chair wrote back to me and the Treasurer and said that perhaps the Energy [Bookmark: Phr19_O=238.1_D=24.6_C=0.85]  Matters Committee might have been a better place for that documentation to go. The Treasurer and I have agreed to provide these documents in their unredacted form on the same condition of confidentiality that was requested of the Public Accounts Committee, so these documents can go to the Energy Matters [Bookmark: Phr20_O=263.4_D=5.3_C=0.75]  Committee and members there have full and  [Bookmark: Phr21_O=270.6_D=23.0_C=0.83] untimed limited access to those.

The Marinus Link Shareholders Agreement allows for disclosure of confidential information for public accountability reasons or as a request from parliament or a parliamentary committee. As a matter of good governance, we would consult with the other shareholders, Victoria and the Commonwealth, in relation to any request to release confidential information. [Bookmark: Phr22_O=294.6_D=5.0_C=0.75]  It's not possible to table the documents here in a public sense without breaching [Bookmark: Phr23_O=300.1_D=4.9_C=0.75]  the confidences of our project Marinus partners and without putting at risk commercial interests of, as I say, Hydro Tasmania - amongst others, but specifically Hydro Tasmania - for whom we have fought very long and hard to keep its information private. I should make the point that those negotiations with the Commonwealth, on the back of keeping that information private, have led to a $346 million grant to TasNetworks for the Northwest Transmission Developments, or a grant against its ratable asset base, which will reduce cost to customers and that's the that's the only intention of that grant. 

It also kept our equity funding of Marinus Link at $103.5 million. I noticed you made some comments around that. That figure - $103.5 million - was the amount of money which had been expended on Project Marinus while it was a wholly owned subsidiary of TasNetworks. As we moved into that tripartite agreement with Victoria and the Commonwealth, we were able to have that $103.5 million recognised as equity contributed. Also, through the negotiation we were able to cap our level of equity into Marinus Link. There's already been a subsequent equity call for Marinus Link and Tasmania hasn't been required to contribute additional equity into that, because our equity is capped and our equity stake in the company reduces over time as further equity calls come.

I would like to be as absolutely open and transparent as I'm able to be, because I see so many benefits from Project Marinus and what it will mean for Tasmania going forward. Energy security is just one of those, and I think it's worth considering that we've just come out of the two driest years on record in terms of hydro inflows. 

Having connectivity into the NEM (National Electricity Market) is so important for us as a state and for our energy security. If you contemplate that Bass Link's already 20-years old and halfway through its service life and that a project like Marinus takes around about 15 years from conception to delivery, we have to have these conversations now about we will do about further connectivity with the NEM.

Having this project delivered by governments as opposed to the private market means that we get all the benefits of the concessional finance rates. Both Marinus Link and the Northwest Transmission Developments - particularly the northwest - are backed by heavily concessional finance from the Clean Energy Finance Corporation, again, the rate of that concessional finance is very tightly held for good reason, and also that very large $346 million federal grant. 

There are a number of reasons why I would seek to have these documents held to be available for people to see them. It is it is a challenging thing to put into the public realm. I would ask members to act in what I would say is the best interests of the state to keep our hydro numbers to ourselves. There are good reasons for doing that amongst some other things. 

I do not support the motion. Thank you.

All that needs to happen is for those claims to be written in all caps and you'd have a Trump-esque tweet to put forward, both in presentation and in hubris. As the member for Mersey has outlined, so I will not go over the ground again in detail, that despite promises to release the whole-of-state business case to the general public 30 days before making its final investment decision, the government failed to do so. [Bookmark: Phr6_O=82.2_D=15.2_C=0.83]  Well, sections of it were released, anyway. This broken promise and sleight-of-hand have escalated the serious disquiet within the community around both the ramifications of Marinus, but also whether they can trust what the government tells them about a project of this magnitude. [Bookmark: Phr7_O=97.9_D=28.8_C=0.81]  People cannot trust that the decision was the best decision made thanks to these public contortions.

What we do know is the government essentially delegated its final investment decision to an expert assessment panel. That panel was comprised of five energy executives. Two were from Hydro Tas and one from TasNetworks. Both of those entities will benefit directly from Marinus going ahead. The expert advisory panel produced its report in July this year. Unsurprisingly, they gave the project the tick of approval and the government followed suit. [Bookmark: Phr8_O=127.5_D=11.1_C=0.75]  Unsurprisingly, the panel's report has been released and it's unredacted. Funny that a glowing report gets released in full. Clearly nothing of concern to see there.

 [Bookmark: Phr9_O=140.6_D=28.8_C=0.77] It's also worth noting the government also relied heavily on a report prepared by the Department of Energy Executive Steering Committee, which is chaired by the Secretary of State Growth. The other members were the Secretary of DPAC, Secretary of Treasury, Secretary of NRE Tas, and acting CEO of ReCFIT. This departmental Energy Executive Steering Committee prepared an 111-page report called the Final Investment Decision Assessment Report and notably, this report is heavily redacted. This is where we see the secrecy surrounding the information [Bookmark: Phr10_O=169.4_D=9.8_C=0.74]  provided to the government upon which it based its final decision, and it begs the question, given the full release of one energy expert panel's report, why the other has been redacted so heavily?

 [Bookmark: Phr15_O=241.6_D=23.8_C=0.79] There are also those pages which have been redacted in their entirety, despite it being arguable these examples of redacted information are in the public interest. This provides another example of the secrecy surrounding this significant project.

 [Bookmark: Phr16_O=266.6_D=4.7_C=0.67] Yes, some of these reductions may be valid on a commercial and confidence basis, but when people are [Bookmark: Phr17_O=271.9_D=22.1_C=0.79]  provided pages which are more blacked out than not, it only serves to undermine and weaken confidence in the application of that assessment.  [Bookmark: Phr18_O=295.4_D=9.6_C=0.77] Moving on to the whole-of-state business case, which is the main subject of this motion, I share the disbelief over the degree of redactions in that release document and the frustration over the manner of its release, which to be frank, felt shady and slippery to say the least; particularly when the whole-of-state business case was promised to be released in a timely manner for the community to inform itself on the decision options which were before the government regarding Project Marinus. 

As we know, that didn't happen. Then, to add insult to injury, a heavily redacted version was released. As the member for Mersey stated: 

No doubt the producers of black toner would have received a boost in sales!

The following summary is of some of the more potentially egregious reductions that may be of interest to members:

On page 60, a large slab of text related to the Northwest Transmission Developments is missing, despite this being expected to cost more than the Macquarie Point Stadium, and the costs for which Tasmanian electricity consumers are solely responsible.

On pages 109 and 110: this redacted section which relates to the wholesale energy cost implications on major industrials, which is highly relevant to Tasmania's economic fortunes. 

Page 170 is entirely redacted. No idea what that relates to; a mystery black page. 

Page 236 provides a graph with the cost axis redacted, so we don't know the cost breakdown of the Marinus scenario versus the no-Marinus scenario, and we are then provided the same redacted graphic on page 239. 

Similarly, the project costs for TasNetworks is redacted on page 253 and page 256, along with the details of how much debt TasNetworks will have to take on, which has been blacked out on page 258. 

Tasmanians might be interested to know what the project costs for Hydro will be, as they appear to run into the billions of dollars on page 277. 

It would be good to know what the funding risks are on page 279. 

In fact, so much information about the details of Hydro's profitability under Project Marinus is so redacted that leaves the reader incapable of making any form of assessment.

I could continue, but those are some of the key areas of removed information and detail from the whole-of-state business case, which, understandably, many in the community feel frustrated and insulted by. 

It's also worth noting this parliament has a joint select committee on energy matters, which has been specifically established. The minister mentioned the committee; it was established at the start of the last parliament to scrutinise and report on the Marinus Link proposal. It's yet to release any report, so it may be that that committee does go into more detail here.

When considering the motion before, I asked myself, 'What is of particular relevance or impact from my electorate of Nelson?' I certainly make no claim to be an energy sector expert by any means. There's clear implications and ramifications of this proposal relevant to all Tasmanians no matter where they are. It clearly has statewide significance beyond the immediate region and electorate which may end up bearing the connector and related infrastructure. The rise and fall and rise again, both in the immediate and long-term, of clean renewable energy supply and costs clearly affect all Tasmanians. It will also affect future Tasmanians, and this project is recognised as having profound intergenerational equity implications for Tasmanians on a range of levels. 

However, it's the secrecy and dissembling of the government on Project Marinus at different stages which this motion specifically seeks to tackle. That is why I felt a responsibility on behalf of my electorate of Nelson and those who raised this issue with me to add my voice to this call, especially in light of the final decision-making stages, key components of which occurred contentiously during the recent snap election's caretaker period, as detailed by the honourable member for Mersey.

Also, within the context of promises given and broken, regarding the release of the whole-of-state business case and the timing in which that release was promised to occur 30 days prior to the finalisation of the final investment decision, to reiterate the honourable member for Mersey's statement: if the project is so important and so positive for Tasmania, then what does the government have to hide? Demonstrate the courage of your conviction and release the detail. Provide the report in full along with the cosigned agreement. Only then will the people of Tasmania have an opportunity to examine whether these projects truly stack up and are truly in the best interest of our state. It would go a long way towards restoring a bit of faith and confidence in how the government goes about its decision-making processes.

I also note that at the 11th hour, once again, the government has circulated, just today, further information regarding concerns about releasing this information as requested by the motion, offering yet another confidential briefing. The government is not reading the room; not the room outside of this parliament's closed committee rooms anyway. It has to get better at working out an accountable framework by which the relevant information can be released or communicated more broadly to the community without always relying on locking up the community's elected representatives to give us exclusive privileged information in order to make pesky democratic accountability motions such as the member for Mersey's go away. 

A select few in the know, being slightly more expanded, is a fairly cold comfort for the concerned citizens who also have a right for the greatest degree of transparency possible. It does not help those landowners, for example, affected by the northwest transmission project. It does not help the people in any electorate worried about the long-term ramifications for our robust and affordable energy network. So, I thank the honourable member for Mersey for providing the chamber with the opportunity to require further transparency from the government on Project Marinus and I support his motion.

[6.21 p.m.]
Ms FORREST (Murchison) - Just for that I'm going to go for an hour and a half.  [Bookmark: Phr3_O=60.1_D=2.6_C=0.48] Mr President, I rise to speak on the motion and with some reservations about supporting it and I will go through my reasons for that.

MOTION

Committee of the Whole Council to consider the State Policies and Projects (Macquarie Point Precinct) Order 2025 (No 49)

[7.57 p.m.]
Ms FORREST (Murchison) - Mr President, I move - 

That the Legislative Council:

Notes on 21 October 2025 the Governor made the State Policies and Projects (Macquarie Point Precinct) Order 2025 (No. 49) (the Order).

Further notes that under section 26(9) of the State Polices and Projects Act 1993 the Order does not have effect until it is approved by a resolution of each House of Parliament.

Resolves that upon any Motion being moved, and Question proposed, that the Council approve the Order (the Motion), so much of Standing Orders be suspended as would prevent:
		upon Debate being closed on the Question proposed by the Motion, but

		before the President puts that Question to the Council, the Council resolving itself into a Committee of the Whole Council for the purpose of considering the Order, and

Empowers that Committee of the Whole Council to consider each Clause, Schedule and Item of the State Policies and Projects (Macquarie Point Precinct) Order 2025 (No. 49) in the following manner:

		the Chair is to call on each Clause, Schedule or Item, and ask if any Member has any question thereon, and

		if no questions are asked, or a question or questions having been asked, no further questions are asked, the Chair shall declare that the Clause, Schedule or Item has been considered,

		the Report of the Committee of the Whole Council be limited to ‘the Committee has considered the State Policies and Projects (Macquarie Point Precinct) Order 2025 (No. 49)’, and

		for the avoidance of doubt, the Committee of the Whole Council is not empowered to amend any Clause, Schedule or Item of the State Policies and Projects (Macquarie Point Precinct) Order 2025 (No. 49).

Further resolves that upon the Report of the Committee of the Whole Council being brought up, the President shall immediately, or at such future time as Ordered by the Council, put the Question proposed by the Motion.

 [Bookmark: Phr11_O=187.0_D=21.2_C=0.80] Mr President, I do not intend to speak for too long on this motion but I think it may be important to step through the process and there has been a few questions asked about that. For those people who may be watching, to understand what this motion seeks to achieve: all of us know over the next two days we will be dealing with the order related to the Macquarie Point Stadium and that is quite a dense and complex document. Because it is an order there is only one vote that can be taken, so every member will have the opportunity to express their thoughts on the order itself, so basically in favour or not of the order, but before you Mr President put the vote after everyone to have the opportunity to speak and the member in charge of the order has the chance to reply to the debate. Once that has occurred, the way this will work is the relevant standing order will be suspended to enable you to move a motion that would enable the House to resolve itself into a committee to consider the order clause-by-clause or section-by-section or item-by-item.  [Bookmark: Phr17_O=263.7_D=28.8_C=0.77] 

That is how the wording of the motion there before you outlines. In that process, it will be a similar process as when we're in the committee stage of a bill and the Deputy Clerk will call each clause or schedule or item and every member will have the opportunity for three questions on that. I will talk more about this if this is approved and we get into that committee-like stage.

 [Bookmark: Phr18_O=293.2_D=11.7_C=0.73] There is just for questions. It's not for re prosecuting the argument of whether we should or should not support the order. It is just to clarify matters in the order related to particular questions on each section. Because we don't have an opportunity to do that without this motion, if we don't support this motion - and that's up to the House, of course - it means that if any members have got questions about the operation of the order or various sections in the order or schedules in the order, they will have to put all their questions in their contribution on the order and it will be up to the leader of the motion to approve the order to be able to respond to all of those questions in one hit without any further clarifying questions. 

This will make it a longer process if it is supported. I would hope that members would consider, if they support this, that they consider their contribution on the principal question of whether the order should be agreed to or not, and then questions be focused in that committee-like stage to enable thorough scrutiny of the order. 

Once we've been through that process, there won't be a question put that the item be agreed to or anything like that; whoever is in the chair will make the declaration that the clause, the schedule or the item has been considered and there won't be a question put. At the end, I will report to you, Mr President - or the Chair will report to you - that the order has been considered, at which time the President will resume his Chair and then put the vote, the one and only vote, on the order itself.

I hope that's clear. If any members have questions, you're quite free to speak on it and I will try to respond to those in my summing up on this. That's the purpose of it. It's not to slow the process down; it's to hopefully make it more rigorous and more like contemporary scrutiny. As we go through, it's easier to ask questions as they relate to a particular provision and move through it that way so that every member has the opportunity to ask questions related to how the order will work in relation to the various parts of that. 

I put that motion. I'm happy to respond to any questions. 

Ms WEBB (Nelson) - Mr President, thank you. I advised to make a brief contribution on this motion which I will be supporting. First, I wish to acknowledge and thank the honourable member for Murchison for bringing this motion to us. It's a clear mechanism for the Chamber to consider. 

Democratic debate is a fundamental principle of any parliament worthy of its name; but democratic debate does not just concern the nature of the debate underway, it is the capacity to even hold a thorough debate on a matter of interest in the first place. That's the significant contribution which this motion makes: it provides a clear and transparent mechanism by which one of the most controversial matters to come before this parliament, I would say, can be considered thoroughly by this Chamber. 

We all know the nature of the statutory instrument, the State Policies and Projects (Macquarie Point Precinct) Order 2025, does not allow for amendment as if it were a bill, but it certainly warrants as thorough an examination as legislation would be awarded.

It's also important that the Legislative Council does uphold the principles of detailed scrutiny as provided for by the framework detailed in the motion: the capacity to consider each clause, schedule and item of the order. Not every clause, schedule or item may require detailed scrutiny, but it is responsible, right and proper for this place to reserve that right and capacity to do so; particularly in light of the fact that the debate limitations which are established under the standing orders of the other place resulted in that debate held on Thursday 13 November, only taking a total of 6.8 hours from 2.44 p.m. to 9.25 p.m. That's 6.8 hours in a Chamber of 35 members on a matter of such intense public interest. That's an observation, not a reflection on the other place, as each Chamber is a master of its own destiny, which I recognise, but I'm just noting it.

We shouldn't seek to make it a competition regarding the length or brevity of debates. We have different roles in the Chambers but, put simply, the order consists of 157 pages. It's a significant statutory instrument and one which will have considerable implications for current and future generations. It was that gravitas and statewide consequence which was used as a rationale in declaring the proposed Macquarie Point stadium a Project of State Significance in the first place and, as such, it deserves and warrants that it is provided the most rigorous and informed scrutiny possible through this place.

This Chamber, as we are occasionally reminded, is the House of review, and this motion is consistent with and delivers on the most important principles of rigorous democratic review and debate in the public interest. 

I support the motion.

[8.05 p.m.]

Ms FORREST (Murchison) -  [Bookmark: Phr16_O=194.8_D=11.4_C=0.85]  [Bookmark: Phr18_O=217.6_D=26.2_C=0.78] Mr. President, I know it's getting late. [Bookmark: Phr19_O=245.2_D=0.6_C=0.90]  It is a matter [Bookmark: Phr20_O=246.1_D=29.9_C=0.74]  that's also before the Public Accounts Committee. He's done a power of work in his office in recent times and really helped to shine a light on some of the failings and failures of governance and process in this state. 

In moving this motion, I wish to address what is [Bookmark: Phr21_O=276.4_D=1.6_C=0.71]  a matter of serious public concern [Bookmark: Phr22_O=279.0_D=16.8_C=0.85] . This is not simply another routine audit report; in fact, as the Auditor-General has said himself, this was an additional performance audit that was conducted after there was more than one, I believe, referral to his office to consider it. [Bookmark: Phr23_O=296.9_D=8.1_C=0.74]  As with all our referrals, whether they come from member of parliament, a member of the Public Accounts Committee, the Tasmanian Audit Office do an assessment and determine whether there's something that should be looked at and whether it should be looked at that time. Obviously there was enough concern raised in the references that he received that he did undertake a review or an audit. 

This is actually a damning indictment of how our public institutions have managed a significant transaction involving publicly-owned land, taxpayer funds and the public interest. I know the member for Elwick, also a member of the PAC, will have a lot more to say in this because this is her backyard and she absolutely understands this probably better than anyone else in this Chamber.

The Auditor-General's findings and conclusion could not be clearer. The Department of State Growth's processes from 2019 to 2025 were inadequate. These processes did not support the identification and effective management of risks associated with the land-sale process and, remarkably, as of September 2025, six years after negotiations commenced, the land-sale process was still not complete.

To be clear about what inadequate means in this context, the Auditor-General found:

First, there was no business case or implementation plan. You wonder how that could happen. Despite the complexity of this proposal involving a basketball team, an entertainment centre upgrade and a significant land transaction, state entities proceeded without the fundamental framework needed to manage the uncertainty, coordinate efforts, and achieve the intended outcomes.

Second, governance arrangements were fundamentally flawed. A steering committee wasn't even established until 17 months after the decision to negotiate. Advice was reactive rather than proactive, with papers marked 'urgent' and 'super urgent'. I mean, surely it's urgent or it's not? Creating an atmosphere where proper due diligence was sacrificed for perceived expediency.

Third, advice to government was insufficient. The Auditor-General found that advice did not clearly identify essential issues, options from implementation, or key risks. This is a fundamental failure of the public service's duty to provide frank, comprehensive and timely advice.

If that's not bad enough, the problems went deeper still. A non-binding sale price of $6 million was publicly released in December 2020, before appropriate valuation processes had been concluded. This is not standard practice. It establishes a reference point that can influence future decisions and negotiations. I think most of us would recognise that is just something that should not have happened. 

Conflicts of interest were not properly managed. They weren't even a standing item in the steering committee meetings until April 2024, five years into the process. The Valuer-General was asked to set a reserve price prematurely based on the draft subdivision plan without being clearly informed a significant change to the plan might alter the market value. Perhaps, most troublingly, a valuation report was shared with the proponent against the explicit advice of the Valuer-General, on the instruction of the Premier's office staff, who, as the Auditor-General notes, are not delegated under legislation to direct public servants to take such actions.

The Auditor-General's report speaks of significant shortcomings in advice and process and failures that state entities must address to maintain public trust. These are not mere administrative quibbles; these are fundamental breakdowns in how the government is supposed to work. 

Over six years, the nature of this proposal has fundamentally changed. What began as a unique unsolicited proposal involving a basketball team, entertainment centre upgrade and sporting facilities all at Wilkinsons Point has morphed into something quite different. The community sports facility moved to another part of Glenorchy. The high-performance facility relocated to Kingston. The uniqueness that justified avoiding an open-market process has fallen away, in the Auditor-General's words.

Yet, throughout these changes, state entities and departmental officials appear to have failed to step back and ask the fundamental questions. Are these remaining works still in Tasmania's interest? That advice wasn't provided until July 2025, six years after the progress had begun.

The Auditor-General has provided 4 clear recommendations:

That the Department of Premier and Cabinet establish protocols to ensure state entities work with ministers in accordance with State Service principles;

That State Growth ensures it provides comprehensive advice on costs, benefit and risks before substantially implementing government commitments

 [Bookmark: Phr2_O=6.1_D=6.2_C=0.74] That State Growth develops a framework to reduce risks in transactions of this nature [Bookmark: Phr3_O=12.8_D=4.2_C=0.57] ; and

That State Growth updates its conflicts of interest policy.

 [Bookmark: Phr4_O=18.3_D=18.8_C=0.81] Mr President, these recommendations have been accepted and I note the response from the Department of State Growth acknowledging improvements since mid 2024, but we must ask, why did it take an Auditor-General's investigation to prompt these basic improvements? This should be good-practice. This should be what was going on anyway, but it was not.

 [Bookmark: Phr5_O=37.5_D=1.0_C=0.95]  [Bookmark: Phr6_O=39.8_D=22.8_C=0.74] I must also note a serious concern raised by the Auditor-General himself. He was restricted from accessing Cabinet documents, preventing him from assessing whether State Growth's implementation was consistent with Cabinet parameters. As the Auditor-General states, this restriction means such investigations not being able to provide a full picture to the Tasmanian parliament. This is unacceptable.

 [Bookmark: Phr7_O=63.6_D=10.2_C=0.81] Parliament and the public deserve a complete picture when public resources and public land are involved.  [Bookmark: Phr23_O=270.5_D=3.7_C=0.63] [8.34 p.m.]
 [Bookmark: Phr24_O=281.3_D=23.7_C=0.83] Ms RATTRAY (McIntyre - Leader for the Government in the Legislative Council) - Mr President, I rise on behalf of the government to make a contribution to this motion regarding the Auditor-General's Report Number 3 2025-26, which examines the proposed sale of Crown land at Wilkinsons Point. 

At the outset, I wish to place on record the government's appreciation for the work of the Auditor-General and his office. Their examination was thorough and detailed, and the government welcomes this scrutiny. Independent oversight such as this strengthens the integrity of our public institutions and ensures continuous improvement in the way government conducts its business.

This proposal has a long history. It arose from an unsolicited proposal received in 2019 that sought to bring together several related initiatives, being a redevelopment of the Derwent Entertainment Centre and the establishment of the Tasmanian NBL team and broader activation of Wilkinsons Point Precinct. At the time, the Government saw a genuine opportunity to revitalise an underutilised area, to encourage private investment and to enhance Tasmania's standing in a national sport and entertainment.

These objectives were and remain in the public interest. The establishment of the JackJumpers team has proven to be an overwhelming success story for our state and the member for Murchison talked about that in her contribution. Since joining the National Basketball League in 2021, the JackJumpers have quickly become a source of immense pride for Tasmanians, uniting communities across the north and the south.

Their remarkable rise culminated in the club's maiden NBL Championship in 2024. The team's achievements have showcased Tasmania's passion for sport and community, elevated our national profile and brought tens of thousands of fans back to the revitalised Derwent Entertainment Centre, now MyState Bank Arena.

Several agreements were entered into over the following years relating to the operation and upgrade of the Derwent Entertainment Centre, sponsorship arrangements and team commitments. However, it is important to note that no sale of land has ever occurred, and no binding agreement is in place. The Crown continues to own both the MyState Bank arena and the surrounding land parcels at Wilkinsons Point.

The Auditor-General's report is comprehensive and candid. It identified a number of areas where improvements could be made in how agencies manage complex land transactions, including valuation timing, communication between departments, and documentation of advice. Importantly, the report does not find any evidence of improper purpose, misconduct or personal benefit. The shortcomings identified are administrative in nature and relate to sequencing, governance and documentation. Matters which are by their very nature capable of being improved. 

The government acknowledges these findings and accepts them in full. The departments involved, the Department of State Growth, Department of Natural Resources and Environment and the Department of Premier and Cabinet have all cooperated fully with the review and have accepted all recommendations.

I'm advised that significant steps have already been taken to implement these recommendations including a strengthened and clearer framework for setting reserve prices for crown land, more robust conflict of interest declaration procedures, revised protocols around sharing of valuation material and unsolicited proposal assessments, and enhanced guidance on interagency coordination when cabinet decisions are implemented.

These improvements have been endorsed by the Secretary's Board and are now being applied consistently across the state service. The government takes very seriously its responsibilities to manage public assets transparently and in the public interest. That is why these findings have been embraced rather than resisted. The government has accepted all recommendations and is actively embedding the lessons from this report into everyday practise.

I'm advised that the Secretary of State Growth and the Secretary of Natural Resources and Environment have each personally overseen the implementation of new procedures to ensure that processes are watertight for any future transactions. It should also be said that the original intent of the proposal was sound and genuine.

 [Bookmark: Phr2_O=18.1_D=24.5_C=0.80] The project sought to unlock significant community and economic benefits through job creation, private investment and a modern sports and entertainment precinct that would enhance Tasmania's national profile. Decisions made at the time were made in good faith and in a fast-moving environment when the opportunities such as securing an NBL franchise for Tasmania [Bookmark: Phr3_O=43.6_D=0.7_C=0.85] , were time critical. [Bookmark: Phr4_O=45.0_D=25.9_C=0.85]  The government's motivation was always to deliver the best outcome for the state.

For clarity and for the benefit of the Chamber, the government advises that no sale of crown land has occurred at Wilkinson Point, and no final decision has been made to dispose of any land. Any decision to proceed will be subject to the full statutory process under the  [Bookmark: Phr5_O=72.0_D=16.9_C=0.80] Crown Lands Act 1976, including an independent valuation by the Valuer-General, the setting of a reserve price by the Director-General of Lands and due consideration of advice through the appropriate processes. [Bookmark: Phr6_O=89.7_D=14.0_C=0.81]  Key public areas including the foreshore, the ferry access area and Loyd Road, are not under consideration for sale. The MyState Bank Arena will remain in public ownership.

 [Bookmark: Phr3_O=31.3_D=12.6_C=0.82] The vessels were to transform Tasmania's tourism capacity and freight capability, and now they sit idle, representing not just direct costs but massive opportunity cost to our economy. [Bookmark: Phr4_O=44.7_D=25.7_C=0.79]  This interim report addresses the two major failures up until December 2024 that converged to create this crisis. 

First, the vessel contracts and the caretaker period issues. The vessel replacement saga began promisingly enough in December 2017. Contracts worth €219 million per vessel [Bookmark: Phr5_O=71.3_D=2.4_C=0.62]  were signed with German shipbuilder, FSG. [Bookmark: Phr6_O=75.1_D=29.4_C=0.75]  I'm not going to try and say the German name because I will butcher it, and FSG will do. When that contract was terminated in this February 2020 due to FSG's financial difficulties, fortunately on this occasion, that occurred before any payments were made so no money was lost in that case. Then TT-Line entered negotiations with the Finnish shipbuilder Rauma Marine Constructions or RMC.

 [Bookmark: Phr7_O=105.8_D=28.0_C=0.79] Following intervention by both the Morrison government demanding increased local content due to the COVID-19 economic concerns, and the state Liberal government at the time under premier Gutwein, a delay occurred when the contract was about to be signed with RMC requiring TT-Line to go back and reinvestigate Australian capacity to build the ships here in Australia. Note here that this work had been done previously [Bookmark: Phr8_O=133.9_D=21.2_C=0.80]  and it had been found that Australia did not have the capacity for this sort of vessel. But the Morrison government wanted to stop this process and perhaps look to Western Australia to see if they could deliver these vessels. This work, as I said, had been done prior to the FSG contract have been entered into.

 [Bookmark: Phr9_O=156.2_D=6.2_C=0.74] That created a delay, and after that delay a new contract was signed with RMC [Bookmark: Phr10_O=163.6_D=9.7_C=0.82]  in April 2021, and that's the same company that they are about to sign a contract for when they were told by the Morrison government to pull it. So they lost their spot in the queue. [Bookmark: Phr11_O=174.4_D=15.9_C=0.85]  In addition to that, the new contract had an additional cost of €25 million with commitments to deliver between AUD50 and AUD100 million in Tasmanian local content. [Bookmark: Phr12_O=191.2_D=25.9_C=0.79]  Then came the force majeure event, Russia's invasion of Ukraine in February 2022, and we're all aware that that event triggered supply chain disruptions, but this was particularly impacted the construction of these two vessels because the steel yard that RMC used to source the steel was bombed by the Russians [Bookmark: Phr13_O=218.4_D=3.5_C=0.70] . Those circumstances resulted in an €8 million [Bookmark: Phr14_O=223.6_D=3.8_C=0.77]  price increase and further delivery delays.

 [Bookmark: Phr15_O=228.8_D=14.0_C=0.80] The truly problematic events occurred during the state's 2024 election caretaker period. On 16 January 2024, RMC advised TT-Line of insufficient liquidity to complete the vessels. [Bookmark: Phr16_O=243.5_D=22.3_C=0.78]  By late February, this requirement had escalated to €100 million across both contracts. What happened next raises serious concerns about governance during caretaker periods. On 14 February 2024, the Premier called an early election for 23 March commencing the caretaker period.
